ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
There is a lack of knowledge regarding chloride ingress under early age exposure condi­
tions of concrete structures and the corresponding effect for the service life. Therefore,
this thesis investigates the impact of early age seawater exposure compared to a normal
curing time of 28 days. The effect of epoxy coating’s ability to reduce the chloride ingress
is also investigated. Furthermore, chloride ingress in concrete cast underwater using the
tremie method is examined.
Three concrete mixes are used: a concrete with CEM III/B (Blast furnace concrete, BFC)
and one with CEM II/A­V (Fly ash concrete, FAC), both with a water­binder (w/b) ratio of
0.40, and a self­compacting underwater concrete (UWC), (w/b) = 0.43, recipe delivered
from a concrete plant. The early age development of FAC and BFC is investigated using
a non­steady­state rapid chloride migration test (RCM).
Concrete elements with different curing times are submerged in a tank filled with 10 °C
seawater for 90 days. After exposure, three chloride profiles for each element are de­
termined for different curing times (1, 2, 4, 7, 14 and 28 days). The apparent diffusion
coefficients and surface concentrations are determined by fitting the profiles to Fick’s 2nd
law solution. A modified version of Fick’s 2nd law is used to make simple predictions of
the long­term effect of early exposure to chlorides.
The BFC offers significantly better resistance against chloride ingress than FAC within the
test period. Analysis of variance shows a significant statistical difference between early
age exposure and exposure after 28 days of curing for FAC and BFC. Compared to FAC
and BFC, the UWC shows significant chloride ingress and possible durability problems
regarding chloride ingress when casting underwater concrete as a bedplate.
The long time effect of early exposure is investigated but contains significant uncertainties,
such as the variation of the apparent diffusion coefficient. The results for the FAC shows
that early age exposure (1 to 4 days) gives a considerable service life reduction between
55 % and 91 % compared to concrete exposed after 28 days of curing. Similarly, early
age exposure for BFC reduces the service life between 84 % and 94 %. For 14 days
curing time before exposure, the reduction was between ­3 % and 68 % for the FAC. For
BFC this was between 22 % and 66 %.
The two investigated epoxy coatings show a significant reduction of the chloride ingress,
thus being a solution for avoiding the negative effects of early age exposure.
The overall conclusion is that early age exposure to chlorides will reduce the service life
significantly.

==PAGE:8==
1
Introduction
The durability of reinforced concrete in marine environments is dominated by corrosion
as the leading deterioration mechanism. Concrete offers natural corrosion protection due
to the alkaline environment of concrete. In marine environments, concrete is subjected to
chloride ions, which penetrates through the concrete and causes premature corrosion due
to pitting. Extensive research has been done in the field of chloride ingress into concrete
due to the increasing focus on durability and the importance of designers’ ability to evalu­
ate the material characteristics. It is essential to have an awareness of the durability with
regard to sustainability. It is estimated that in the industrially developed countries, about
40 % of the total resources of the construction industry is due to repair and maintenance
of existing structures [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014].
1.1
Case studies of construction methods with early age
exposure
The construction industry is constantly changing, and new constructions schemes are
introduced to increase the efficiency in the process. Therefore, the designers need an in­
creased understanding of evaluating the schemes concerning durability. Some projects,
such as Portier Cove in Monaco and the Thames River flood barrier in London, are ex­
posed to marine environments immediately or early after casting. There is a lack of un­
derstanding and limited research of early age exposure to chlorides of young concrete
structures.
1.1.1
Portier Cove, Monaco
The Portier Cove project in the Principality of Monaco is a reclamation scheme that adds
60,000 m2 of land to the territory and is currently under construction and expected to be
delivered by 2025. The construction technique for the marine structures consists of a
fill enclosed by 18 trapezoidal reinforced concrete caissons, 27 meters tall and weighing
10,000 tons each [Cremona et al., 2019]. The design specifications of the caissons are
100­years in terms of durability.
Figure 1.1: Left: Overview and assembly of the caissons in Porter Cove, Monaco, be­
fore landfill. Right: The semisubmersible ”caissonnier” barge Marco Polo in the port of
Marseille [Cremona et al., 2019].
The concrete caissons will be precast both onshore and offshore, where the offshore
worksite is a 56 meter wide and 27 meter tall caissons, see fig. 1.1, used to fabricate the
1
==PAGE:9==
elements. This construction scheme leads to early age exposure of concrete since it is
desired to speed up the construction process, which requires exposure of the caissons’
rafts after five days and the walls after three days. This unusual early age exposure
is covered in a durability design and assessment by a probabilistic performance­based
design and compliance approach in [Cremona et al., 2019] using [“fib Bulletin 34. Model
code for service life design”, 2006]. The results are highlighted in the state­of­the­art
report presented in the literature study in chapter 2.
1.1.2
Thames River flood barrier, London
The Thames River flood barrier was constructed in 1982 to prevent tidal floods in the Lon­
don area. The foundation project consisted of constructing nine piers with access to the
piers from a jetty. Underwater excavations and concrete placement directly in seawater
using the tremie method was necessary. The underwater concrete for each pier base was
completed using tremie pipes, transporting 7,000 tons of concrete for five days without
breaks [Peterson and Lee, 2004]. The method is shown in fig. 1.2.
Figure 1.2: Left: Thames River flood barrier gates. Right: Underwater foundation con­
struction sequence [Peterson and Lee, 2004].
There are many examples of deep foundations cast directly in seawater using tremie
concrete. However, the durability aspect of the early age exposure regarding chloride
ingress is not fully enlightened to the authors’ knowledge.
1.2
Scope
The focus of this master’s thesis is to investigate the chloride ingress into concrete under
early age exposure conditions at 10 °C and assess the effect regarding the long time
durability.
Three different types of concrete recipes are used: A CEM II/A­V recipe (Fly ash concrete,
FAC), CEM III/B recipe (Blast furnace concrete, BFC) and a self­compacting underwater
concrete (UWC).
The following subjects are investigated in this thesis:
• How the chloride ingress and the apparent diffusion coefficient are influenced for
submerged concrete (FAC and BFC) subjected by early age exposure to seawater.
Curing times before exposure are 1, 2, 4, 7, 14 and 28 days.
• Durability assessment of underwater concrete (UWC) cast directly in seawater using
the tremie method. It is assessed for a bedplate exposed at moment the of casting
and two wall elements demoulded under water respectively after 1 and 7 days.
• Determine the development of concrete permeability at an early age by performing
a rapid chloride migration (RCM) experiment at different ages between 2 to 92 days
for FAC and BFC.
2
==PAGE:10==
• Assessment of the long time durability regarding chloride ingress in concrete ex­
posed early with simplified diffusion models.
• Investigate the effect of concrete epoxy coating applied as early as possible with
regards to minimise chloride ingress.
The thesis will not focus on:
• Developing a model for the durability regarding chloride ingress of concrete sub­
jected to early age exposure. However, simplified diffusion models will be used to
assess the long­term effect of early age exposure.
1.3
Approach for the study
It has been of interest to simulate realistic conditions for seawater in Oresund, Denmark,
with an average seawater temperature of 10 °C and seawater collected in Danish wa­
ters. However, the use of seawater cause unavoidable uncertainties which may not have
occurred with artificial seawater.
The choice of cement is based on conventional uses in aggressive environments for in­
frastructure projects in Denmark and the rest of Europe. Therefore, the concrete mix
design has a water/binder ratio of 0.4 and is based on high­performance concretes used
for the Fehmarn Belt Fixed Link. The mix design for the self­compacting concrete used for
underwater casting is based on a thoroughly tested recipe from a Danish concrete sup­
plier (Unicon A/S). Therefore, this study has not focused on developing chloride­resistant
mix designs but is based on designs used in the industry.
Assessment of the long term effect of early age exposure is based on an engineering
approach. This thesis is not about developing an advanced model for predicting chloride
ingress but highlighting the effect of early age exposure as tendencies. Simplified models
for diffusion have been used, and the authors are aware of more advanced models which
may give more reliable results. The model used for this project is thoroughly discussed,
and a sensitivity analysis is performed to illustrate the uncertainties within the field of
chloride ingress modelling. Furthermore, statistical measures have been used to quantify
the confidence intervals of the obtained results and the conclusions made are therefore
assessed well­founded.
1.4
Thesis structure
Present master’s thesis starts with a literature study working as a state­of­the­art report
within early age exposure of concrete structures. The relevant theory regarding concrete
and chloride transport mechanisms, along with modelling of chloride ingress, is presented.
Afterwards, a description of conducted experiments is given, followed by the correspond­
ing results. Based on the results, a discussion of the obtained chloride profiles and con­
crete development is used to predict the reduction of service life when exposed early age.
The effect of epoxy coating is also evaluated. The thesis ends with a conclusion, further
work and comprehensive appendices containing detailed experiment descriptions, raw
data, photo documentation and additional reports conducted during the master’s thesis.
3
==PAGE:11==
2
Literature study
The present study has been conducted due to a lack of previous work in early age expo­
sure on concrete structures. The following articles highlight the research area and work
as a ’state­of­the­art’ (STAR) report.
2.1
Bagheri, Alireza, A. Ajam, and H. Zanganeh (2019)
Bagheri, Alireza, A. Ajam, and H. Zanganeh (2019). “Investigation of chloride ingress
into concrete under very early age exposure conditions” in the journal Construction and
Building Materials is an experimental study investigating the time variation of the diffusion
coefficient and surface chloride values for both normal age and very early age exposures.
The used concrete mix consists of a CEM II with a cement content of 420 kg/m3. Different
w/c ratios are investigated, including w/c = 0.4. An RCM test [NT BUILD 492] and a natural
diffusion by immersion test are performed [NT BUILD 443], where the latter uses a NaCl
solution with a concentration of 16.5 %.
Table 2.1 shows the non­steady­state migration coefficient determined by the RCM test.
The study performs an RCM test of a specimen cured one day, but this is not possible
within the framework of [NT BUILD 492] since the test takes at least 48 hours to perform.
The study does not contain any explanation in this regard, and it is unclear which method
the study has used.

apparent diffusion coefficients for 0­180, 3­180 and 28­180 are of particular interest. The
nomenclature used in the study is, for example, that ”3­180” denotes that the specimen
has cured to the age of 3 days and been exposed to the age of 180 days, i.e. exposure
time is 177 days. This means that the exposure time is not identical between the different
specimens.
The early exposure to chlorides results in higher apparent diffusion coefficients than the
concrete exposed later. It is unknown if the apparent diffusion coefficients in table 2.2 are
from single specimens or an average value of more specimens. No standard deviation
is documented, and [NT BUILD 443] prescribes that a minimum of 3 specimens for each
series should be used. Regardless, [NT BUILD 443] describes that the variation coeffi­
cient (the standard deviation divided by the mean value) can be expected to be 20 % for
the surface concentration and 15 % for the apparent diffusion coefficient. Given these
4

study are within each other interval of variation. Therefore, the conclusions should be

2.2
Cremona, Christian et al. (2019)
Cremona, Christian et al. (2019). ”Durability analysis of the maritime infrastructure for the
Monaco Sea extension” in Structural Concrete 20 describes the design approach for the
”Portier Cove” project in the Principality of Monaco.
The concrete design was designed according to fib model code (Service Life Design), [“fib
Bulletin 34. Model code for service life design”, 2006]. The study investigated the chloride
diffusion coefficient for concrete exposed to either seawater or NaCl solution after 3­28
days of curing. The investigated cement was a CEM V/A 42,5 N PM ES CP1. The cement
comprises 60 % of OPC, 20 % ground granulated blast furnace slag (GGBS) and 20 % of
pulverised fly ash (PFA). The water/binder ratio and the mix design are not specified.
The study uses cylindrical specimens with epoxy coating to obtain one­directional diffu­
sion. Two series are investigated: Seawater from the Marseille harbour and an artificially
saline solution of either 31 g/L or 21.4 g/L sodium chloride. It is not specified which artifi­
cially saline solution is used. The study also included a test series where the specimens
are covered with the epoxy coating BASF MasterSeal M338, which is also tested in the
present thesis.
Note: The following paragraphs highlighting the results obtained by the study should be
used with careful considerations. The article is not thoroughly described, and therefore
faulty reproduction of data could be probable.
Figure 2.2 shows the fitted chlorides profiles at 56 and 91 days. For (a), (b), only the 3­day
specimen is coated and compared to 5 and 28 days non­coated specimens. In the figure,
(c), (d) are all non­coated concrete specimens. It is not specified whether the chloride
content is free chloride (water­soluble) or total chlorides (acid­soluble).

Figure 2.2: Comparison between fitted chloride content profiles. In (a) and (b) ONLY the
3 days specimen is coated with BASF MasterSeal M338, (c) and (d) none are coated.

The study shows that for the non­coated specimens after 91 days exposure, fig. 2.2(d),
28 days curing time gives less chloride ingress compared to early age exposure. All
profiles were obtained at 91 days. Therefore, the concrete with 3 and 28 days curing time
was exposed to the saline solution for 88 and 63 days, respectively. The study does not
address this observation.
The study finds that for the coated specimens exposed after 3 days with 91 days exposure,
fig. 2.2(a), has less chloride ingress than the reference 28 days uncoated specimen. The
chloride ingress is, however, approximately equal. The uncoated 5 days curing period
shows more chloride ingress than the reference 28 days uncoated specimen.
The obtained chloride diffusion coefficients and non­steady­state migration coefficients
from the results are presented in table 2.4. The values are obtained by fitting the solution of
the nonlinear least square problem to Fick’s 2nd law. Since Fick’s 2nd law is not applicable
for coated concrete, it is unclear whether this has been taken into account or ignored in the
article. The 28 days concrete does have the highest diffusion coefficient when exposed
to seawater (free chloride), according to the study.

Caballero, J. et al. (2012)
Caballero, J. et al. (2012). “Chloride penetration into cementitious mortar at early age” in

specimens. Both an RCM test, [NT BUILD 492], and a diffusion test based on [NT BUILD

2.4
Ben Frej, Amor et al. (2019)
Ben Fraj, Amor et al. (2019). “Investigating the early­age diffusion of chloride ions in
hardening slag­blended mortars on the light of their hydration progress” in Construction
and Building Materials investigates the ingress of chlorides during the hardening of slag­
blended mortars that have been exposed to chlorides at an early age. The study investi­
gates different slag­blended mortars with 0 % to 60 % slag.
The specimens are cured for 24 hours in a mould, then demoulded and directly exposed
to chlorides for 1, 7, 14 and 28 days. The chloride diffusion is performed with an alkaline
solution containing 30 g/L of NaCl. The study also investigates the evolution of non­
evaporate water in the materials using thermo­gravimetric analysis (TGA) at 1, 7, 14 and
28 days.
10
==PAGE:18==
Figure 2.5: Chloride profiles of C40BFS60S1, [Ben Fraj et al., 2019]. The concrete con­
sists of 40 % CEM I 52.5 N PM ES, 60 % blast furnace slag and a Sand/binder = 1.
Figure 2.5 shows one of the investigated chloride profiles. The profile shows that approx­
imately 40 % of the chloride ingress into the mortar will be achieved during one day of
exposure compared to an exposure time of 28 day. The surface concentration increases
from 0.34 % to 0.52 %. The progress for the apparent diffusion coefficient and bound
water can be seen in fig. 2.6, where the black line (C100BFS00S1) is a reference mortar
without slag. This indicates the status of the hydration process with respect to the chloride
ingress.
Figure 2.6: Evolution of apparent diffusion coefficient, Da (or Dapp), and bound water, Wb,
of different mortars [Ben Fraj et al., 2019].
The study concluded that as the hydration progresses, the microstructure becomes more
refined and obstructs the chloride diffusion paths. They also conclude that increased
content of slag particles improves the hydration reaction and resistance against chlorides.
11
==PAGE:19==
2.5
STAR summary
It is difficult to draw a joint conclusion based on the four articles presented in this state­
of­the­art report. Chloride diffusion and migration properties are highly dependant on the
exposure conditions and material properties for each specific case, and direct compari­
son is therefore not possible. However, all four articles present that concrete undergoes
rapid development in the early ages, leading to increasing chloride ingress compared to
concrete exposed after 28 days of curing. Different approaches for modelling the effect on
service life are proposed with varying results on the effect of early age exposure, varying
from a service life reduction from 10 % to 34 %. However, the tendencies are identical
between the articles.
The literature research conducted as a part of this state­of­the­art report has highlighted
the need for further research within the topic of early age exposure of concrete structures
to chloride ingress. The presented articles are of varying quality and are, to the knowledge
of the authors of this thesis, the only available material within the topic.
12
==PAGE:20==
3
Theory
3.1
Concrete and cement
Developing a suitable concrete recipe for aggressive environments is of great importance
regarding the durability of the concrete. The ingress of chlorides is one of the mechanisms
that can lead to durability problems in aggressive environments. The chloride ingress can
be reduced by using different mineral admixtures like fly ash, ground granulated blast­
furnace slag etc. [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014].
3.1.1
Fly ash cement
Fly ash (FA) cement is a combined cement product where some cement is replaced with
FA. Fly ash is produced under the combustion process in a thermal power plant. Fly ash
is categorised into different classes, which depend upon chemical properties. Likewise,
will the physical properties of fly ash depend on where the fly ash is produced and the
used fuel in the power plant [Gamage et al., 2011].
FA addition benefits the compressive strength, sulfate resistance and reduces the rebar
corrosion because it lowers the chloride permeability relative to ordinary Portland cement
concrete. The use of FA in concrete will generally reduce the freeze­thaw resistance [Shi
et al., 2012].
3.1.2
Ground granulated blast furnace slag
Ground granulated blast furnace slag (GGBFS) is a byproduct of iron and steel production.
The cement is partly replaced with GGBFS, which will reduce the compressive strength,
but the chloride diffusivity is reduced significantly, thus increasing chloride resistance.
GGBFS addition regarding sulfate resistance is complex and very dependent on the re­
placement level and the composition. The addition will also reduce the pH of the pore
solution but still increase the binding capacity regarding chlorides [Shi et al., 2012].
3.2
Corrosion
Corrosion of the steel reinforcement in concrete structures is a major durability problem.
The corrosion of steel occurs since it is thermodynamically favourable. Steel is a pro­
cessed product with a higher energy level than its original state as iron ore. The lower
energy level is more stable than the higher level, and therefore the material will try to lower
its energy level. The process to achieve this lower level is corrosion [Bardal, 2004]. The
corrosion of a divalent metal M in an aqueous solution is shown in fig. 3.1. The process
consists of a cathodic and anodic reaction. The anodic reaction (oxidation) starts with the
metal being dissolved and transferred to the solution as metal ions, M2+. The cathodic
reaction in this example is the reduction of oxygen to hydroxide ions. The metal and hy­
droxide will form a metal hydroxide that may be deposited. Corrosion of steel/iron will
create Fe(OH)2, which is not stable and with oxygen and water it will oxidize to hydrated
iron oxide, Fe2O3 · nH2O or other products depending the conditions.
13
==PAGE:21==
Anodic reaction
e-
Cathodic reaction
Aqueous solution
Figure 3.1: Corrosion of a divalent metal M in an electrolyte containing oxygen.
The pH is an essential variable regarding the equilibrium of both the anodic and cathodic
reactions. The pore water solution in concrete will typically have a high pH since the sur­
plus of Ca(OH)2. The high pH creates conditions for passivity of the steel reinforcement.
The passivised metal will typically reduce the corrosion rate significantly and, as a result,
effectively ’stop’ the corrosion process. This passivisation is vulnerable to the introduction
of chlorides, which leads to pitting corrosion. The chlorides shift the thermodynamically
and equilibrium conditions and make it possible for the steel to corrode [Bardal, 2004].
The corrosion compromises the structural integrity of the structure by reducing the sec­
tion area of the reinforcement and causing concrete cracks due to corrosion products
being expansive [Frederiksen, 2013].
3.2.1
Critical chloride content
The critical chloride content, Ccrit, in concrete is the threshold value for which the depassi­
vation of the steel occurs. Reliable critical chloride contents are important for determining
the service life, and it has been identified as one of the most decisive input parameters.
The critical chloride content is typically limited to or around 0.4 % by weight of the cement
[Angst et al., 2009; Shi et al., 2012; DS/EN 206]. The critical chloride content can be used
as a stochastic variable in probabilistic modelling. An example is [“fib Bulletin 34. Model
code for service life design”, 2006], where it follows a beta­distribution with a mean value
of 0.6 % by weight of cement. In this thesis, the critical chloride content is used as 0.1 %
weight of dry concrete.
3.3
Chloride transportation mechanisms
The transportation of chloride ions into concrete are mainly governed by three mecha­
nisms: Diffusion, permeability and absorption. Pure diffusion occurs due to difference in
the concentration of ions. The ions will be transported from the part with highest concen­
tration to the part with lowest concentration [Nilsson et al., 1996]. Permeability is driven
by the pressure gradient. The flow of water and hydrostatic pressure of water will both
create a pressure gradient [Boddy et al., 1999]. The absorption mechanism is the trans­
port of liquids in the pore system due to surface tension. This effect will only happen if the
concrete has been dried to some extent [Nilsson et al., 1996].
The chlorides in concrete can be categorised by two types: Free and bound chlorides.
The free chlorides are free ions dissolved in the pore solution and the bound chlorides are
either chemically or physically bound. [Nilsson et al., 1996].
3.3.1
Binding
The binding of chloride in concrete is an important mechanism since it removes chloride
ions from the concrete pore solution into the solid hydrated binder phase. It is believed
14
==PAGE:22==
that only free chloride ions can initiate reinforcement corrosion, and therefore concrete
with higher binding capacity have better durability against corrosion [Nilsson et al., 1996].
The binding of chlorides occurs as both chemical and physical binding. In Portland ce­
ment, chlorides can be chemically bound with the calcium­aluminate hydrates as AFm
hydrates denoted Friedel’s salt. The chlorides can also be physically adsorbed on the
C­S­H. [Nilsson et al., 1996; Justnes, 1997; Florea and Brouwers, 2012].
3.3.2
Diffusion
Pure diffusion of ions in the pore solution occurs due to the concentration difference of
the ions. The diffusion of ions can be described by Fick’s 1st Law, see eq. (3.1).
F = −D ∂c
∂x
(3.1)
F
is the flow of ions in [kg/m2s].
D
is the diffusion coefficient [m2/s] according to Fick’s law.
∂c
∂x
is the chloride concentration gradient with respect to the depth.
Equation (3.1) describes the steady­state conditions, which can be achieved by a diffusion
cell or migration test. The movement of the chlorides ion in concrete is not pure diffusion.
Other ions will also interfere with the diffusion of chlorides, and the composition of the
pore solution will also change over time because of the solubility and binding of different
substances. Since the conditions change with time, the chloride flow will not be constant.
Fick’s 2nd law describes this, see eq. (3.2).
∂c
∂t = D ∂2c
∂x2
(3.2)
It is important to remember that the diffusion coefficient in eq. (3.2) is constant contrary
to the properties of a diffusion coefficient in concrete, which is time dependant [Nilsson
et al., 1996]. Fick’s 2nd law does not describe chloride transport perfectly since it neglects
several physical and chemical phenomenons. The law describes concrete as a perfectly
homogenous material, which is not the case for several reasons. Firstly, the binder content
profile is changing due to the wall effect of aggregates and secondly, the formation of
an interfacial transition zone paste­aggregate boundaries, but this has been shown in
[Jensen et al., 1999] to not significantly affect diffusion rates.
The solution to the partial differential equation, eq. (3.2), with the left boundary condition to
be a constant concentration, C(x=0, t) = Cs, and the concentration is zero at the beginning,
C(x, t=0) = 0, will be a common error function solution, see eq. (3.3).
C(x, t) = Cs

With the use of eq. (3.3) a chloride profile can be fitted to it, and a diffusion coefficient and
surface concentration can be obtained. [DS/EN 12390­11] use this method and calls the
obtained diffusion coefficient the non­steady­state diffusion coefficient.
15
==PAGE:23==
Nomenclature for D values
The naming of the diffusion can create some confusion since there are different nomen­
clature for D. In order to create some clarity the following nomenclature and definitions
will be used in this thesis:
Ds
the steady­state diffusion coefficient [m2/s]
Dinst
the instantaneous non­steady diffusion coefficient [m2/s]
Dnss
the non­steady­state diffusion [m2/s]
Dnssm
the non­steady­state migration coefficient [m2/s]
Dapp
the apparent diffusion coefficient [m2/s] obtained by fitting eq. (3.3)
to test specimens and with [Cl­ expressed in % of concrete weight].
Dapp(t)
the apparent diffusion coefficient extrapolated as function of time [m2/s]
The present thesis use Dapp for the diffusion experiment and Dnssm for the rapid chloride
migration experiment.
Diffusion dependency of temperature
The diffusion of chloride is dependent on temperature. Higher temperatures will result
in a higher diffusion rate of chlorides [Oh and Jang, 2007; Song, Lee, and Ann, 2008].
The concrete is exposed at an early age, and therefore it will not have fully hydrated
yet. Furthermore, the diffusion coefficient is a function of hydration. [Bagheri, Ajam, and
Zanganeh, 2019; Caballero et al., 2012] Therefore, the hydration progress will affect the
total diffusion [Nilsson et al., 1996]. The process of hydration of cement is coupled with
the temperature and is shown in fig. 3.2. The relative maturity at 10 °C is 0.62 (Exposure
conditions in this thesis).
Figure 3.2: Relative maturity as function of temperature, relative maturity for 10 °C com­
pared to 20 °C is 0.62 [Vestergaard Nielsen, 2013]
.
Fig. 3.2 is created using Arrhenius equation, see eq. (3.4), where 20 °C is defined as
index 1.0. The diffusion dependency of temperature can also be described by eq. (3.4)
as a factor. Life­365™Service Life Prediction Model™use this relationship to account for
the temperature­dependt change, see eq. (3.5), [Life­365 Consortium III, 2020].

Surface concentration
The surface concentration is the obtained value from fitting eq. (3.3) to a measured chlo­
ride profile. The problem with the determination of Cs is that it represents the chloride
content at the exposed surface. The difficulty arises due to the reduced chloride content
at the surface because of the skin effect. Therefore, the conditions for exposure have a
significant impact on the surface Cs. When concrete is submerged in concrete, it can be
assumed that the concrete and seawater will eventually obtain an equilibrium. Studies
show that no clear relationship can be determined with only exposure time since various
factors affect it. Some of these factors are binder type, free water/binder ratio (w/b), curing
conditions, weathering etc. [Song, Lee, and Ann, 2008].
The surface concentration is a time­dependent variable, and several different models to
describe its development is found in the literature [Song, Lee, and Ann, 2008; Nilsson et
al., 1996; Bagheri, Ajam, and Zanganeh, 2019; Luping and Gulikers, 2007]. Regardless,
the modelling in section 6.5 assumes a constant Cs.
3.4
Modelling of chloride ingress
The service life of a structure is of vital importance in most civil engineering projects.
Some projects prescribe a service life of more than 100 years, and therefore it is im­
practical to make an exact experiment replica to determine the service life. However, it
is possible to model the service life and base the model on experiments and data from
existing structures. Many different models have been created over the decades, e.g. Life­
365, DuraCrete, and ClincConc. Some of the models use probabilistic modelling, others
are numerical solutions. In addition, some models use dozens of parameters. However,
there is no ’perfect’ model.
This thesis is not about chloride modelling, but its use is necessary for estimating the effect
of early exposure. Therefore, a practical engineering approach is used, and a simple
model is chosen.
17
==PAGE:25==
3.4.1
Fick’s 2nd law
The ingress of chlorides is assumed to obey the modified Fick’s 2nd law of diffusion,
eq. (3.6). This model will be used to estimate the chloride ingress.

The surface chloride content is assumed to be constant and the apparent diffusion coef­
ficient will be modelled as time­dependent.
3.4.2
Time dependency of diffusion coefficients
The apparent diffusion coefficient is, as mentioned, not constant and will be reduced over
time. This ageing can be estimated by eq. (3.7). This ageing of the diffusion coefficient is
described in several studies [Bagheri, Ajam, and Zanganeh, 2019; Life­365 Consortium
III, 2020; Frederiksen, Mejlbro, and Nillson, 2008; Luping and Gulikers, 2007] and also
fib bulletin 34 ­ Model code for Service Life Design.

(3.7)
Dapp(t0)
is the apparent diffusion coefficient in [m2/s] measured at reference time t0.
α
is the age factor [­]
The age factor describes the ageing of concrete, which affects the diffusion coefficient over
time. This factor is determined by fitting data from real structures to eq. (3.7). Several
studies have shown it is difficult to estimate the age factor. Likewise this factor is one
of the most important factors regarding service life [Andrade, Castellote, and D’Andrea,
2011; Baroghel­Bouny et al., 2013; Gaal, Koenders, and Polder, 2006].

[“fib Bulletin 34. Model code for service life design”, 2006].

==PAGE:26==
4
Experiments
The present study aims at determining the effect of early age exposure and consists of
a main experiment and some secondary investigations. In fig. 4.1 a principle for nomen­
clature in this project is shown, and in fig. 4.2 an experiment overview is expressed as a
flow diagram.
The main experiment consists of exposing concrete elements to seawater after 1, 2, 4, 7,
14 and 28 days of curing. The seawater is temperature­controlled at 10 °C. After 90 days
of exposure to seawater, the chloride profile’s apparent diffusion coefficients and surface
concentrations are determined for each curing period.
Three types of concretes are investigated:
• Fly ash concrete (FAC)
• Blast furnace concrete (BFC)
• Underwater concrete (UWC) or self­compacting­concrete
The UWC is cast directly underwater using the tremie method and also contains fly ash
cement, but are named differently for clarity, see section 4.2 for mix design. The UWC is
exposed after 1 and 7 days for two cast wall elements and after 0 days (immediately) for
a bedplate.
As a quality control for the concrete in the main experiment, air content, density, and slump
measurement is controlled for the fresh concrete and the compression strength at 28
maturity days. After exposure to seawater, the elements are sawn, and the homogeneity
of the cross­section is visually controlled. Furthermore, the cross­section is sprayed with
silver nitrate to control the chloride front for irregularities.
The first secondary investigation is to determine the effect of two different epoxy impreg­
nation products; SIKA Sikagard®­Wallcoat N and BASF MasterSeal M 338. The epoxy
impregnation is investigated for 2 and 28 days curing periods before 90 days seawater ex­
posure. For the 28 day curing period, the product is applied as prescribed by the product
datasheet.
Another parameter to determine the development of concrete resistance against chloride
ingress is a rapid chloride migration (RCM) test. Here an electrical field is applied for typi­
cally 24 hours, and the chloride ingress is used to determine a non­steady­state migration
coefficient. The test is conducted on 2, 4, 7, 14, 28, 59, and 92 maturity days of concrete.

==PAGE:29==
4.1.2
Cement
Two types of cement has been chosen for this study;
• CEM II/A­V 52,5 N (Aalborg Portland A/S)
• CEM III/B 32,5 N­SR (OYAK Cement)
According to the manufacturer, the Portland­fly ash cement (CEM II/A­V 52,5 N) from
Aalborg Portland A/S has a siliceous fly ash content of 16%, see appendix F.1.
The sulphate resisting ground granulated blast furnace slag cement (CEM III/B 32,5 N­
SR) from OYAK Cement contains 66­80% ground­granulated blast­furnace slag. Accord­
ing to an analysis report from the manufacturer the chloride content is 0.018 %, see ap­
pendix F.2.
These cement types were used to create a realistic scenario for chloride ingress in marine
structures. Both types are of high quality and have relative high resistance against chloride
ingress.
4.1.3
Epoxy coating products
It is of interest to investigate the use of a epoxy coating to minimise the effect of early age
seawater exposure. This project will investigate the efficiency, regarding early exposure,
of two different water­borne rigid epoxy coatings for concrete exposed after 2 and 28 days.
• SIKA: Sikagard®­Wallcoat N
• BASF: MasterSeal M 338
The principle and schedule for applying the epoxy products are seen in section 4.6.
SIKA: Sikagard®­Wallcoat N
The Sikagard®­Wallcoat N by SIKA is a 2­component, coloured, water dispersed epoxy
product. The manufacturer states that its field of application is concrete and other cemen­
titious materials on indoor wall elements. They furthermore state that it has good chemical
resistance and mechanical resistance, good adherence to moist surfaces, water vapour
open etc. For more details, the product datasheet is found in appendix F.3.
BASF: MasterSeal M 338
The MasterSeal M 338 from BASF is water­based epoxy coating for waterproofing and
protection of concrete elements. The manufacturer states that its application is concrete
and other cementitious materials for indoor and outdoor use. They write that it is for
protection for pipes, canals, tanks, drains, retaining wall, bridge decks, tunnels and for
buildings. They state it has good chemical resistance and good adherence to moist sur­
faces. When the material is cured, it is diffusion closed water and carbon dioxide, but
water vapour open. For more details, the product datasheet is found in appendix F.4.
22
==PAGE:30==
4.2

The mix design for both FAC and BFC is based on test mix designs for The Fehmarnbelt
Fixed Link project [Per Aarsleff A/S, personal communication, 2021]. The Fehmarnbelt
Fixed Link project mix designs use 380 kg/m3 cement and a (w/b)=0.38. This project use
400kg/m3 cement and a (w/b)=0.4 to improve the workability. The used aggregates for
all recipes are categorised as extra aggressive, E, which is the best class in Denmark.
The mix design for FAC is shown in table 4.2, BFC in table 4.3 and UWC in table 4.4.
The water content in the concrete is adjusted regarding the water content in the additives,
e.g. plasticiser. The moisture content in the aggregates is determined as described in
appendix A.2.

==PAGE:32==
4.3
Mixing and casting of concrete
This study consists of exposing three different concrete mixes for seawater with two differ­
ent casting techniques. FAC and BFC will be cast using traditional measures and curing
conditions, where UWC is cast directly underwater using the tremie method.
4.3.1
FAC and BFC
The early age exposure of concrete is by this study tested for FAC and BFC with different
curing times of 1, 2, 4, 7, 14 and 28 days. Afterwards, the concrete elements are exposed
to seawater for 90 days. The FAC and BFC concrete is mixed, poured, vibrated and cured
by the same procedure. A detailed experiment description is found in appendix A.3.
The concrete elements, which should be exposed for 90 days in seawater, is cast in
phenolic film faced plywood moulds with a dimension of 150x220x460 mm, as shown
in fig. 4.5. The dimension is chosen for three pc. of Ø100 mm cores could be drilled
and still have enough distance to the edges so that one­dimensional chloride transport
is achieved. There is a 60 mm distance to the edges, which is more than the expected
chloride ingress.
460 mm
220 mm
150 mm
Ø100
Ø100
Ø100
60 mm
20 mm
Figure 4.5: Dimension of concrete elements used in this study.
Firstly the moulds (150x220x460 mm elements and Ø100x200 mm cylinders) are pre­
pared and applied with a form releasing agent. The concrete mix design is adjusted ac­
cording to the water content in the aggregates, see appendix B.2.
Constituents according to table 4.2 or 4.3 is prepared and the aggregates are filled in the
Zyklos rotating pan mixer. The aggregates are dry­mixed for 30 seconds. While mixing
continues, the water is added, followed by the superplasticizer. The concrete is mixed
for approximately 3 minutes. The properties of the fresh concrete are documented as
described in section 4.4.
The concrete moulds are filled with fresh concrete and vibrated at 50 Hz as described
in appendix A.3. The cylinders are closed with the associated lids and fastened with a
clamp. After 24 hours, the cylinders are demoulded and placed in a curing tank filled with
tap water at 20 °C ± 2 °C for 28 days or the desired curing age for RCM cylinders.
The concrete element moulds are closed with a plastic cover held down by metal plates.
The concrete is left for curing in a temperature­controlled room at 20°C ± 2 °C for 24
hours. Afterwards, the concrete element is demoulded and wrapped in a plastic cover
tightly secured by duct tape. Note that the element should only cure 24 hours before
seawater exposure is not plastic­wrapped after demoulding. The curing continues in the
temperature­controlled room until the desired curing age is reached (1, 2, 4, 7, 14 or 28
days). After reaching the desired curing time, the plastic is removed, and the concrete is
placed in the seawater tanks, which are temperature controlled at 10 °C for 90 days.
25
==PAGE:33==
4.3.2
UWC
This study investigates the effect of early age exposure, and it is therefore relevant to
include the so­called underwater concrete (UWC) (also referred to as self­compacting
concrete) cast by the tremie method. The concept is to directly cast the concrete under­
water using a tremie pipe placed at the bottom. The concrete is poured into the pipe,
and with the pipe constantly placed below the concrete level, the poured concrete will
disperse the water with little to no mixture between the water and concrete, except for
the upper sludge layer. The European Federation of Foundation Contractors (EFFC) and
Deep Foundations Institute (DFI) has, by a joint concrete task group, created ”EFFC/DFI
Best Practice Guide to Tremie Concrete for Deep Foundations”, which have been the
basis for the execution of UWC made in this study.
The most critical part of placing tremie concrete is the initiation, where it is essential to
ensure that the first load of concrete is separated from the water. The first stages of the
tremie pour can be seen in fig. 4.6. Here the concrete and the fluid is initially separated
with a separation medium. This project uses inflatable rubber balls with a diameter of 55
mm. These prevent the initial pour of concrete from mixing with the water, which would
lead to segregation.
Where
hf
Fluid level in excavation
DT
Diameter of tremie pipe
D
Dimension of excavation
db­t
Distance from bottom of excavation to tremie pipe outlet
hc
Concrete level in excavation
hc,T
Concrete level in tremie pipe (hydrostatic balance point)
h1/h2
Embedment of tremie pipe before (1) / after (2) tremie pipe cut
sT
Section length of tremie pipe section to cut
p0/pi
Hydrostatic pressure outside (o) / inside (i) of excavation
pi,T
Hydrostatic pressure inside the tremie pipe
Figure 4.6: Phases in the tremie pour sequence with pressure conditions, figure taken
from [Guide to Tremie Concrete for Deep Foundations, 2018].
The concrete flow is based on the pressure conditions marked in fig. 4.6. The weight of the
concrete in the tremie pipe must overcome the hydrostatic fluid pressure, the resistance of
the already poured concrete and the friction inside the tremie pipe. This pressure condition
is also referred to as the hydrostatic balance point.
It is essential to keep the tremie pipe embedded within the concrete to avoid further segre­
gation. For structures, it is, in general, recommended that a minimum embedment should
be 3 meters [Guide to Tremie Concrete for Deep Foundations, 2018]. This limit can not
be used for this study, where it is necessary to maintain the casting on a laboratory scale.
The size of the water tank (pallet tank) and the element cast for this study can be seen in
the experimental setup shown in fig. 4.7.
26
==PAGE:34==
Pallet tank,
H = 1000 mm
Bedplate

Experiment setup
500 mm
300 mm
300 mm
Figure 4.7: Experimental setup for UWC experiment with two wall elements and one
bedplate placed in a pallet tank. The red circles indicates the location of drilled cores
after end exposure.
It has been chosen to investigate 3 cases (two wall elements and one bedplate) for this
study:
• Wall element which is demoulded after 1 day.
• Wall element which is demoulded after 7 days.
• Bedplate which will have immediate contact with seawater from the moment of cast­
ing, i.e. 0 days.
Furthermore, a reference specimen is cast after the principle of section 4.3.1 (without
vibration) and cured for one day before seawater exposure. This investigates the direct
consequence for casting with the tremie principle instead of a standard casting method.
Cylinders for compression strength were also cast.
Based on initial experiments conducted to get experience with the casting techniques of
tremie concrete, it was concluded that it is complicated to control the casting sequence on
a small scale if the pallet tank is filled with seawater at casting time. Instead, each of the
moulds were filled with seawater, and the tremie concrete would then displace the water
to the empty pallet tank. In this way, the ”dirty” water can also be removed, and new fresh
seawater can be added, which would simulate more realistic conditions. The drawback of
this method is the lack of hydrostatic pressure from the water level. Nevertheless, since
the difference from the top of the mould to the water level in the pallet tank is a maximum of
300 mm, it would not be possible with the current setup to create a significant hydrostatic
pressure as seen when casting tremie concrete at a construction site.
In fig. 4.8 a principle for the laboratory scale tremie casting can be seen followed with
a detailed step­by­step explanation. The tremie tube used (PVC) has a diameter of 110
mm. As the hopper, a large masonry bucket is used with a hole for the tremie tube.
The concrete was delivered by a concrete truck, where the concrete was spun at high
speed for 5 minutes. The concrete was poured from the truck into a container. From the
container, a suitable number of 20 L buckets were filled with concrete. Before casting the
slump flow was controlled according to section 4.4.4.
27
==PAGE:35==
t = 5 min
t = 0 min

Figure 4.8: Principle for casting UWC
NB: In the following, the term ”concrete is poured” refers to pouring a 20 L bucket directly
into the tube. The hopper is not filled with concrete, but used to get concrete into the
tube without spilling. Initial trials found that this was the best way to control the casting
sequence.
Step 1
Pallet tank is empty. Mould with form releasing agent is placed in pallet tank and
mould is filled with 10 °C seawater. Hopper and tremie tube is positioned (by crane)
in the bottom of the mould, with a gap of 2 cm. The tube is filled with ten inflatable
rubber balls.
Step 2
Concrete is poured into the tremie tube, and the balls are pushed downwards. Sea­
water is pushed out of the pipe, and the balls will eventually be trapped at the bottom
of the tube.
Step 3
The tube is lifted just enough to let the balls float out. The concrete will now float
and cover the bottom of the tube.
Step 4
Concrete pouring continues, and the tremie pipe is slowly lifted at small steps to
alter the hydrostatic balance point.
Step 5
The mould is barely filled with concrete, leaving a few centimetres of seawater on
top. Step 1 to 5 is repeated for the remaining elements.
Step 6
The tube and hopper is removed, and the pallet tank is cleaned from the contami­
nated water.
Step 7­9
The pallet tank is now being filled with 10 °C fresh seawater
Step 10
The pallet tank is full of fresh seawater, and the concrete cures in the mould.
Step 11
After 1 or 7 days, the mould is removed, and chloride penetration into the sides can
begin. The moulds are constructed so that they can be demoulded underwater. This
step is neglected for the bedplate, which is chloride exposed from the top and the
mould is therefore not removed.

4.4
Properties of fresh concrete
The fresh concrete used in this study will be subject to quality control regarding checking
the air content, density and slump (­flow). These properties will be used to check the
uniformity of the batches.
4.4.1
Air content
The air content of the fresh concrete is determined based on the standard [DS/EN 12350­
7]. Detailed description of experiment procedure is found in appendix A.6.
4.4.2
Density
The density of the fresh concrete is determined based on the standard [DS/EN 12350­6].
The experiment deviates from the standard regarding the density container used. For
simplicity, the air test container used in appendix A.6 is used. A detailed description of
the experiment procedure is found in appendix A.7.
4.4.3
Slump
The slump measurement test of the fresh concrete (FAC and BFC) is determined based
on the standard [DS/EN 12350­2]. A detailed description of experiment procedure is found
in appendix A.4.
4.4.4
Slump Flow
The slump flow measurement of the UWC is determined based on the standard [DS/EN
12350­8]. The baseplate material (phenolic film faced plywood) deviates from the stan­
dard, which uses a steel plate. Detailed description of experiment procedure is found in
appendix A.5.
4.5
Compressive strength
The quality and consistency of the concrete used for this project are, amongst other mea­
sures, quantified by testing the compressive strength based on [DS/EN 12390­3].
For each concrete batch mixed, three cylinders of Ø100x200 mm were cast. The cylinders
were then water cured in tap water at 20 °C ± 2 °C for 28 days. Afterwards, it was tested
in the TONY 3000 compression testing machine as shown in fig. 4.12, with parameters
based on [DS/EN 12390­3]. Details of the experiment are found in appendix A.9.
Figure 4.12: TONY 3000 compression testing machine conforming to [DS/EN 12390­3].
30
==PAGE:38==
4.6
Application of epoxy products
The epoxy products described in section 4.1.3 are applied to the concrete with two layers.
Both manufacturers state an identical layer thickness for each layer, and the two products
are therefore suitable for comparison. The layer thickness for the first layer is 200 g/m2,
whereas it is 250 g/m2 for the second layer.
The main difference between the two products in terms of the application procedure is
that the first layer of BASF MasterSeal M338 is diluted with 10 % tap water, whereas the
SIKA Sikagaard­Wallcoat N is diluted with 5 % tap water. Both products are 2­component
epoxy and are therefore to be mixed as stated by the product datasheet at the given ratio
between component A and component B.
After the desired curing period, the plastic cover is removed from the concrete. It has
been decided that excess form oil should not be removed to simulate realistic conditions
in the industry.
Before applying the epoxy product, the surface moisture content is measured with a sur­
face moisture indicator (NOVANEX) and reported. The radiation of the indicator pene­
trates the surface under test to a depth of approximately 4 cm. Furthermore, a more de­
tailed analysis of the moisture content of the concrete has been conducted, where a large
sample has been oven­dried to determine the moisture content. Refer to appendix B.2
for details.
The concrete specimens are placed in a fume hood, fig. 4.13, and the epoxy product
is mixed until a homogeneous mixture is achieved (approximately 3 min). The epoxy is
applied with a brush.
Figure 4.13: Epoxy covered specimens hardening in a fume hood. Left specimen is FAC­
2d­BASF, whereas the white are FAC­2/28d­SIKA.
In order to control the layer thickness, the mixed epoxy and the container and brush are
continuously weighed. Based on the layer thickness and the surface area of each side,
the amount of epoxy applied is calculated.
Details of the application procedure are found in appendix A.8.
31
==PAGE:39==
4.7
Exposure conditions
This project aims at simulating realistic conditions found in marine environments in Den­
mark. Both the exposure water and temperature has been aimed at conditions found in
Oresund. The present section describes how these conditions have been met.
4.7.1
Seawater from Oresund
The seawater is from Oresund, more precise retrieved at Taarbæk Harbour. The location
is marked in fig. 4.14.
Figure 4.14: Location of site (Taarbæk Harbour) where seawater has been collected.
Exact coordinates for placement of submersible pump: 55◦47’13.5”N 12◦35’45.3”E.
The water was pumped by a submersible pump from outside the wharf. This was in order
to minimise potential brackish water from the inside of the harbour. Outside the wharf,
there are also a current, which ensures that the water’s composition (mineral/salt content)
corresponds to Oresund in general.
Due to leaching of calcium hydroxide of the exposed concrete, it is necessary to constantly
monitor the collected seawater and change it when needed. The leaching of calcium
hydroxide will alter the seawater’s pH value, and this parameter has been chosen for
assessing when it is necessary to change the water. Fresh seawater has been measured
as having an approximate pH value of 8.5, and a pH value of 9.5 has been chosen to be
the limit.
Based on the pH limit of 9.5, it has been necessary to change and therefore collect new
seawater four times from 15.01.2021 to 12.04.2021. The flow in Oresund changes over
time, and the salinity/chloride content of the seawater is therefore changing. Since the
casting dates for the elements are different for obvious practical reasons, exposure to
different chloride contents is also different. In fig. 4.15 there is an overview at which times
the concrete elements have been exposed to each of the four collections days (runs).

4.7.2
Temperature control
The average temperature of Oresund in the Copenhagen area is 9.7 °C with a maximum
monthly average of 19 °C in August and a minimum monthly average of 1.8 °C in March
[“Climate data for Copenhagen (1999­2019)”, 2019]. Since the temperature affects the
hydration products and time for the early exposed concrete, the choice of exposure tem­
perature does have a significant effect on the results obtained by this study. Due to the
relatively short duration of exposure in this study (90 days), it is not meaningful to have
a season control based temperature approach. In addition, to reduce the number of vari­
ables that could affect the chloride ingress, it has been chosen to have a constant tem­
perature. Previous studies about early age exposure of concrete to marine environments,
as presented in chapter 2, uses a temperature of 20 °C. Therefore, it was of interest to
see the effect of early age exposure in conditions found in Denmark, and the average
temperature of Oresund has therefore been used (10 °C).
The seawater collected at Oresund is stored in temperature­controlled curing tank with
constant recirculating as shown in fig. 4.16.
Cooling water IN
Cooling water OUT
Recirculation basin
Heating element
with temperature
control and stirrer
Isolated curing
basin with seawater
capable of holding 8
concrete elements
Pump for 
recirculation
Figure 4.16: Curing basin setup with temperature control and constant recirculation.
Due to the size of the concrete elements used for underwater concrete castings, it was
impossible to use the isolated curing basins. Instead, a pallet tank has been cut open
and connected to the cooling system in the same way as the curing basins. Since it is not
isolated, the inlet temperature was set lower than 10 °C due to temperature loss to the
environment. The temperature of the pallet tank and curing tank has been continuously
monitored with an externally verified thermometer, and the temperature has remained
constant within 10 °C ± 1 °C.

==PAGE:42==
4.8
Determination of chloride profile
The main result from this study is the chloride profiles obtained from the seawater exposed
concrete elements described in 4.3 (FAC, BFC and UWC). The chloride profiles are ob­
tained from 3 drilled Ø100 mm cores in each element according to [DS/EN 12390­11].
Grinding equipment (dry­grinder) is used to obtain powder samples from specific depths.
Each of the powder samples are then acid dissolved, and the chloride content is deter­
mined by potentiometric titration. Selected cylinders are also analysed for water­soluble
chloride content. The chloride profile is obtained based on the retrieved chloride content
from which the apparent diffusion coefficient and surface concentration is calculated.
A detailed step­by­step description of experiment equipment, chemicals and procedure
can be found in appendix A.10 and A.11. A principle diagram for the experiment can be
seen in 4.17.
CURING AT 20 °C
for 1, 2, 4, 7 14 or 28 days
MIXING
FAC, BFC, UWC
CASTING
Elements and
UWC elements
SEAWATER EXPOSURE
at 10°C for 90 days

of concrete powder
Figure 4.17: Principle of determining chloride profile of exposed elements. Detailed de­
scription are found in appendix A.10 and B.1.
4.8.1
Drilling and grinding
The elements are cured for the desired period and then exposed to seawater for 90 days
at 10 °C. The drilling of the Ø100 mm cores is performed with a water­cooled diamond drill.
The setup is shown in fig. 4.18. Immediately after drilling the cores, the grinding should
start to avoid additional movement of the chlorides. A detailed procedure for drilling and
grinding are found in appendix A.10.
Figure 4.18: Left: Core drilling. Centre: Grinding machine and powder collection box.
Right: Powder collection box with lid.
A part of this thesis has been to develop the method used at DTU Civil Engineering for
35
==PAGE:43==
grinding and obtaining the powder samples. Since the grinding equipment is dry­grinding,
much dust is developed, and strong ventilation is required for health concerns. However,
a lot of fine particles were observed to be lost to the ventilation system. Therefore, both
in terms of health concerns and in terms of method accuracy, the authors developed the
closed collecting box, which can be seen in fig. 4.18.
According to the standard, [DS/EN 12390­11], eight grinding intervals should be mea­
sured. Based on the titration setup at DTU Civil Engineering and for further accuracy, it
was chosen to grind nine intervals for each drilled core. Based on the expected maximum
chloride ingress, a suitable grinding interval is chosen from table 2 in [DS/EN 12390­11].
Since it can be challenging to predict the ingress, some of the chloride profiles obtained
by the present study have more than one background value.
4.8.2
Sample preparation
The chloride content of the concrete powder obtained by grinding is determined as the
acid­soluble content based on the method described by [DS/EN 14629]. Due to the early
age exposure of concrete, it is also interesting to determine the water­soluble chloride
content to assess the binding ratio. The water­soluble chloride content is determined for
FAC and BFC for the curing times of 1, 7 and 28 days only. The water­soluble chloride
content is determined based on the method described in [ASTM C1218].
The powder samples obtained by the grinding procedure are dried in a ventilated oven at
105 °C until constant mass to determine the dry sample mass before titration.
Acid­soluble chloride content
The procedure for sample preparation in order to obtain the acid­soluble chloride content
is described in appendix A.11. Firstly, demineralised water and concentrated nitric acid
are added to the concrete powder. Afterwards, boiling demineralised water is added, and
the content is thoroughly shaken, and nitric acid is added until gas evolution is no longer
observed. The solution is filtered into a clean glass bottle and mixed with demineralised
water to a total sample volume of 200 mL.
Water­soluble chloride content
The procedure for sample preparation in order to obtain the acid­soluble chloride content
is described in appendix A.11. The concrete powder is mixed with 50 mL of boiling dem­
ineralised water and allowed to stand for 24 hours. Afterwards, the solution is filtered into
a clean glass bottle and mixed with concentrated nitric acid and hot demineralised water.
Cold demineralised water is added to a total sample volume of 200 mL.
4.8.3
Potentiometric titration
The principle for potentiometric titration is identical for both the acid and water­soluble
chloride content.
The potentiometric titration is performed with the Metrohm 888 Titrando system using the
Tiamo 2.5 software. The analysis is performed using the first derivative method. Small
amounts of titrant, 0.1M AgNO3, is added to the dissolved sample while measuring the
potential changes via the electrode. The endpoint is calculated as the maximum change
in potential reading per volume of the titrant. The result shown in the Tiamo 2.5 software
as presented in fig. 4.19. The endpoint is used to translate to the volume of chloride ions
in the solution. The amount of silver nitrate used to reach the endpoint corresponds 1:1
(volume) to the volume of chlorides present in the sample solution.
36
==PAGE:44==
Figure 4.19: Example of titration curve output from the Tiamo 2.5 software controlling the
Metrohm Titrando 888 titration equipment. The shown sample is one of the cylinders of
BFC­7d at 0­1 mm grinding interval.
The used electrode is a combined silver ring electrode with a pH glass membrane as a
reference electrode. Silver nitrate forms sparingly soluble precipitates with several anions
and will show more than one equivalence point if other than chloride ions are present. The
anion with the lowest solubility product Kl will precipitate first. The solubility product of
different ions is shown in table 4.6, showing that silver chloride has the highest solubility.
For both the acid and water­soluble chloride content, 1 mL of 0.1 M sodium chloride so­
lution is added to increase the accuracy of the potentiometric titration at low chloride con­
centrations. If there is no chloride in the sample solution, measurements will only produce
half of the S­shaped curve, and it is not possible to identify the point corresponding to the
disappearance of chloride by numerical differentiation.
DTU Civil Engineering acquired the Titrando 888 machine with the software Tiamo 2.5
at the beginning of this project. Therefore, a part of this thesis has been to set up the
machinery and create a user manual for the new software (not appended to this thesis).
Furthermore, methodical analysis of the accuracy of the titrator has been made. The
manual and a report of the accuracy are found in appendix B.1. The authors found the
accuracy of the methods satisfactory with a relative standard deviation of 0.364 % of a 5
mL sample (corresponding to an expected chloride content in a sample used for general
titration of concrete powder).
Table 4.6: Solubility product of different anions, (Metrohm, 2021)
Ag salt
Solubility product
[mol3/L3]

1.56 10­10
Ag2CO3
6.15 10­12
AgBr
7.7 10­13
AgI
1.5 10­16

1.6 10­49

4.9
Visual assessment of chloride front
As a quality check and as a qualitative measure for the consistency of the chloride front of
the submerged concrete elements, a visual assessment of the chloride front is performed.
After the cores are drilled (as described in section 4.8.1) the element is sawn, as shown
in fig. 4.20 and 4.21, in a water­cooled diamond saw. The sawn surface is air­dried until
no moisture is visible on the surface.
50 mm
Sawn surface
Position of drilled 
Ø100 mm cylinders
Figure 4.20: Principle of sawn surface on concrete
elements
Figure 4.21:
Concrete el­
ement sawn in the water
cooled diamond saw
Before the chloride front is assessed, the sawn section is used to check for the concrete’s
homogeneity (visual assessment) as an additional quality parameter for the study. The
surface is photographed.
The surface is sprayed with a 0.1 M AgNO3 solution, which will, after drying, show a
chloride front. In fig. 4.22 some of the freshly sprayed sections can be seen.
Figure 4.22: Spraying of 0.1 M silver nitrate on sawn concete element surfaces.
The chloride front is photographed and will be discussed in chapter 6. It is essential to
notice that the chloride front observed by this experiment cannot be directly translated to
the results obtained by the chloride profile for several reasons: The chloride profiles are
obtained as the acid­soluble chloride content, whereas the visual front is the free chloride
content. Furthermore, there is a difference in how much the FAC and BFC concrete will
bind the chlorides. Another factor is that the water­cooled diamond saw will wash the
surface, and the front where the silver nitrate will reach will therefore be affected. This
experiment can, therefore, only be used for checking the inhomogeneity of the chloride
front.
38
==PAGE:46==
4.10
Rapid chloride migration (RCM) test
In order to determine the non­steady­state (nss) migration coefficient, the principle of rapid
chloride migration (RCM) test is performed according to [NT BUILD 492]. The method
principle is to apply an external electrical potential and thereby force the chloride ions
in the cathode solution (10% NaCl) to migrate into the specimen. After a specific test
duration, the specimen is axially split, and a silver nitrate solution is used to measure the
chloride penetration depth. This depth is used to translate to a non­steady­state (nss)
migration coefficient.
A detailed step­by­step description of experiment equipment, chemicals and procedure
can be found in appendix A.12. A principle diagram for the experiment can be seen in
fig. 4.23.
CURING AT 20 °C
for 2, 4, 7 14, 28, 59
or 92 days
MIXING
FAC, BFC
CASTING
Cylinders, Ø100x200
SAWING
3 x 50 mm slices for each
cylinder
PRECONDITIONING
Vacuum (3h),
Ca(OH)2 saturation (21h)
MIGRATION TEST
Electrical field over specimen
SPLIT SPECIMEN
by axial compression
CHLORIDE FRONT
Sprayed 0.1M AgNO 3
on split section
CALCULATE Dnssm
Figure 4.23: Principle of RCM test. Detailed description are found in appendix A.12.
The purpose of using this test in this project is to see the development of the migration
coefficient and to determine the age factor for the two different types of concrete (FAC
and BFC). The RCM test has not been performed on the UWC.
The test is performed after 2, 4, 7, 14, 28, 59 and 92 days of curing. The concrete is cast
into Ø100x200 mm cylinders ­ one for each curing day / concrete type, i.e. 14 cylinders
in total. The cylinders are water cured at 20 °C.
Twenty­four hours before desired curing age is reached, the cylinders are cut with a water­
cooled diamond saw into three slices of 50 mm ± 2 mm slices. The casting surface, which
contains relative more cement paste, is removed, see fig. 4.24.
200 mm
50 mm
A
B
C
Figure 4.24: Cutting of RCM cylinder into 50 mm slices, denoted A, B and C. The arrows
indicate which side is exposed to the chloride solution (catholyte).
39
==PAGE:47==
The three slices are marked with A, B and C and arrows as indicated in fig. 4.24 and placed
in a vacuum desiccator for preconditioning of the specimens. The absolute pressure in
the vacuum desiccator is reduced in the range of 10­50 mbar. The vacuum is maintained
for three hours, and then, while maintaining the vacuum, a saturated Ca(OH)2 solution is
added, so it immerses the specimens. The vacuum is maintained for an additional hour
before the air is allowed to re­enter the desiccator. The specimens are kept in the solution
for 20 hours, i.e. the total preconditioning time is 24 hours.
The RCM­setup is seen in fig. 4.25. The catholyte solution is 10% NaCl (2 M), and the
anolyte solution is 0.3 M NaOH.
Figure 4.25: Princple of RCM­setup. a = rubber sleve, b = anolyte, c = anode, d = speci­
men, e = catholyte, f = cathode, g = plastic supper, h = plastic box [NT BUILD 492].
An external electrical potential is applied, and based on the resistance measurement of
the specimens, the necessary voltage and test duration is determined based on fig. A.12
in appendix A.12.
Figure 4.26: Left: RCM setup with power supply. Center: Axially splitting of specimen in
TONY 3000. Right: Chloride front after termination of experiment (FAC­92d­A).
After termination of the test, the specimen is axially split as shown in fig. 4.26. A 0.1 M
silver nitrate solution is sprayed on the freshly split section, and when the white silver
chloride precipitation is visible, the chloride front can be measured. The chloride front is
measured at intervals of 10 mm to obtain seven depths. The outer 10 mm in each side is
left out in order to avoid edge effects.
The average value of the penetration depth is used to calculate the non­steady­state mi­
gration coefficient (Dnssm) as described by [NT BUILD 492]. The equation can be found
in eq. (A.6) in appendix A.12.

5
Results
The present chapter outlines the results obtained by conducting the experiments de­
scribed in chapter 4. The results are displayed as the raw data of the experiments. Further
analysis of the data will be discussed in chapter 6.
The chapter will highlight the fresh concrete properties followed by the compressive strength.
Afterwards, the chloride ingress obtained from the 19 concrete elements (FAC, BFC and
UWC) submerged 90 days in seawater are presented. At first by a visual chloride front
followed by three chloride profiles (acid­soluble) for each concrete element. For each
chloride profile, the apparent diffusion coefficients and surface concentrations have been
calculated and are presented. In addition to the acid­soluble chloride profile, the water­
soluble chloride profile is presented for FAC and BFC for selected profiles (1, 7 and 28
days curing time). At last, the non­steady­state migration coefficient found by the rapid
chloride migration experiment is presented.

5.3
Diffusion test
The present section outlines the main results of this study for chloride ingress of the con­
crete, which have been submerged into 10 °C seawater for 90 days. The chloride ingress
has been determined with three different approaches; visual assessment of chloride front,
acid­soluble chloride profile and water­soluble chloride profiles.
5.3.1
Visual assessment of homogeneity and chloride front
The chloride front is found as described in section 4.9 and photographed for each of the
concrete elements used in the study. In the photo shown in fig. 5.6, an example of the
chloride front can be seen for the element FAC­1d. The corresponding photography for
each element are to be found in appendix C.1.
Figure 5.6: Example of visual chloride front of element ”FAC­1d”. Appendix C.1 shows
extensive photo documentation of each concrete element in this study.
5.3.2
Chloride profile (acid soluble)
The following paragraphs show the chloride profiles obtained from the seawater exposed
concrete elements. The axis limits are maintained constant within each concrete type for
a clear comparison between each profile. The chloride profile and detailed measurements
for each drilled core are found in appendix D.3.1.
For each drilled core, calculations for the apparent diffusion coefficients and the surface
concentrations are made. These results are tabulated in the following paragraphs. Here
the average and standard deviation are made from the individual calculated core. De­
tails for the calculations are also found in appendix D.3.1. The surface concentration is
expressed in terms of [% d.c.m.], which is [% dry concrete mass].
The initial chloride content, which is used for the calculation of the apparent diffusion
coefficient, is determined as an average value of background measurements for concrete
type FAC and BFC and shown in table 5.1. For UWC, the initial chloride content used for
the calculation must be found by each casting, due to the risk in the casting technique
where seawater could be mixed in the concrete. The value showed for UWC in table 5.1
is the average of all UWC castings but is not used for further calculations.
Table 5.1: Initial chloride concentrations for each concrete type. Values are found as an
average of background values and shown with standard deviations.

The first measurement point closest to the concrete surface is by default discarded for the
regression analysis as prescribed by [DS/EN 12390­11]. However, where it is assessed
reasonable more than one of the first data points have been discarded. In addition, the
regression is continued until the first data point reaches a value between initial chloride
concentration, Ci, and Ci + 0.015%. This is called the ’zero­point’. Layers deeper than
the zero point are excluded from the regression analysis. The disregarded data­points for
regression analysis are highlighted in appendix D.3.1.
The suitability of the chosen mathematical model against the available dataset is verified
by the coefficient of determination (R2), which must be larger than 0.950. The coefficient of
determination is reported in appendix D.3.1 for each profile. For all cases in this study, the
coefficient of determination is above the limit. The minimum coefficient of determination
is 0.996 for both FAC and BFC, whereas the minimum for UWC is 0.986.

5.4
Rapid chloride migration (RCM)
The rapid chloride migration test has been conducted to determine the time variation of
the non­steady­state migration coefficient for both FAC and BFC. The values found based
on the experiment described in section 5.4 are found in fig. 5.33. For each curing time
(2, 4, 7, 18, 28, 59 and 92 days) and concrete type (FAC and BFC), the non­steady­
state migration coefficient has been determined for three samples, and the results shown
are the average coefficient. The standard deviation is shown as an error bar for each
measuring point.

Figure 5.33: Time variation of the non­steady­state migration coefficient for FAC and BFC.
The grey error bars represents the standard deviation.
For tabulated results and data obtained for the result, refer to appendix D.2.
For both investigated concretes, a rapid drop in the early ages (2­7 days) is observed,
with a 35% reduction for FAC and 61% for BFC, which continues to decrease until a mod­
erate reduction is observed around 18 days. Compared to 2 days values, the reduction
decrease, from 18 days to 28 days, is 9% for FAC and 2% for BFC.
53
==PAGE:61==
6
Discussion
In this chapter, the concrete quality and experiment uncertainties are addressed at first.
The rapid chloride migration results will be assessed and used to discuss the development
of the concrete and determine an age factor. This is followed by comparing the chloride
profiles obtained by the diffusion test and compared to tendencies found in the literature.
Chloride binding and temperature is discussed afterwards.
Based on the experimentally determined chloride profiles, Fick’s 2nd law of diffusion is
used to determine the apparent diffusion coefficients and surface concentrations. The
obtained results will be discussed and assessed by statistical measures. Based on the
obtained apparent diffusion coefficients and surface concentrations, a chloride ingress
model for extrapolations of the results will be used to determine the effect of early age
exposure. This model is thoroughly assessed by a sensitivity analysis.
The effect of epoxy coating will be assessed based on a comparative measurement of the
chloride ingress into uncoated references.
The results obtained from the experiment with UWC are decoupled and written in a sep­
arate section for a more explicit discussion.
6.1
Concrete properties
Researching within concrete technology leads to inevitable uncertainties due to uncontrol­
lable variety in the concrete properties. Therefore it is important to document the quality
of the concrete, and for this study, the concrete has been checked at its fresh stage for air
content, density, and slump. The hardened concrete has been checked by compressive
strength and a visual assessment of the homogeneity and chloride front.
6.1.1
Fresh properties
The measured fresh properties are air content, density, and slump measurement. Fig­
ure 5.1, fig. 5.2 and fig. 5.3 shows the values obtained for the fresh properties. All values
are within two times the standard deviation, and therefore it is concluded that no outliers
are measured. Likewise, it is seen that there is no direct correlation between the fresh
properties and the obtained apparent diffusion coefficient.
6.1.2
Compressive strength
The compressive strength is a measure of strength and is also linked to the pore structure
of the concrete, which is of importance concerning transport mechanisms. Concrete with
low porosity usually has high strength and high resistance to chloride ingress, [CCAA,
2009]. It is, therefore, crucial to the conclusions of this study that the concrete com­
pressive strength is constant within a concrete group (FAC/BFC). The reported values for
compressive strength in fig. 5.5 shows that within each concrete group, the measured
compressive strength is within two standard deviations for all concrete mixes. The effect
with regards to affecting the transport mechanism is considered negligible and will not be
discussed further.
6.1.3
Visual assessment of homogeneity and chloride front
The elements submerged in seawater are sawn, and a visual assessment of homogeneity
has been performed. After this, the sawn sides were sprayed with 0.1 M silver nitrate to
observe the chloride front. The chloride fronts are used to see any local differences in
chloride ingress compared to the overall front. Extensive photo documentation are located
54
==PAGE:62==
in appendix C. None of the elements shows extensive inhomogeneity in the aggregate
distribution or significant local difference in the chloride front.
The chloride fronts show a small variation of the chloride penetration as a function of
depth. This emphasizes why the use of more cylinders than one is preferable. Likewise,
it is concluded that there is no relation between chloride front depth and actual chloride
depth determined by titration. This is probably due to the use of cooling water when
sawing and also the method in general.
6.2
Experiment uncertainties
The experiments and methods will inevitably introduce uncertainties regarding factors that
could affect chloride ingress. The following paragraphs will examine and discuss the most
relevant.
6.2.1
Exposure and curing conditions
The exposure conditions for the submerged test elements have not been constant be­
cause of the change of seawater, minor temperature deviations, and flow patterns around
the elements.
Salinity
The salinity is, as mentioned earlier, not constant. The seawater had a pH of approx­
imately 8.5. Due to leaching of calcium hydroxide, the exposure water will eventually
increase in pH. It has been chosen to use the pH value of 9.5 as the limit where the water
is changed. The schedule is shown in fig. 4.15.
It has been necessary to collect new seawater four times during the project period. Due
to natural variation of salinity in Oresund, four different salinity levels have therefore also
been used, see fig. 4.15. There is no significant difference between run two and run three.
Figure 6.1 shows the exposure conditions for FAC­1d and FAC­14d and also the average
level of salinity. The average salinity is calculated as a weighted average. FAC­1d have
been exposed to an average salinity of 15.02 g/L. FAC­14d to an average salinity of 15.89
g/L. FAC­1d and FAC­14d are the elements with the most considerable difference.
Days after 
exposure
FAC-1d
g/L
FAC-14d
g/L
Avg. 
salinity
15.02
