60
70
80
90
10
20
30
40
50
Figure 6.1: Excerpt from fig. 4.15. The figure shows the exposure conditions for FAC­1d
and FAC­14d and the average salinity they are exposed to.
This comparison is not perfect for estimating the chloride exposure since FAC­1d has
significantly less resistance against chloride ingress at the first ten days compared to FAC­
14d. The first seawater exposure for FAC­1d (green) in fig. 6.1 has a salinity of 16.2 g/L
compared to the red with 11.04 g/L for FAC­14d. Since the salinity and apparent diffusion
coefficient coupling are not linear, it is difficult to estimate this difference in exposure. In
this example, it must be estimated that FAC­1d has been ’more’ exposed to chlorides than
FAC­14d.
The influence of this is difficult to quantify and is therefore not treated more. Likewise,
it was the intention to obtain as equal salinity as possible, but the salinity variations in
Oresund were underestimated.
Temperature
The temperature of both the curing room and seawater exposure tanks have minor tem­
perature variations. Regarding the curing, it is estimated that the room had a constant
55
==PAGE:63==
temperature of 20 °C ± 2 °C, which corresponds with curing standards. Nevertheless, it is
known that maturity is influenced by the exothermic reactions from the hydration process.
The temperature development was measured by casting thermocouples in the concrete
elements. Due to power outage, this information were lost. It were noticed that the con­
crete surfaces of both FAC and BFC were warm on the surface one day after casting,
but was not noticed after two days. Due to the temperature increase, it is impossible to
determine the accurate maturity days.
The exposure tanks had a temperature variation determined to be 10 °C ± 1 °C. The
variation of ±1 °C at 10 °C corresponds to approximately ± 5% in the rate of maturity and
rate of diffusion. This variation is not practically possible to introduce, and the influence
of temperature variation is neglected in further discussion.
Flow
The water flow around the elements can create a positive pressure gradient, increasing
the chloride ingress and influencing diffusion determination. The exposure tank has a flow
from the intake and outlet, but the water flow is low, and as a result of this, the pressure is
also low. It is assumed that the flow of water is negligible regarding increasing the chloride
ingress. Nevertheless, it is believed that the flow will create a mixing of the water and give
a more homogeneous solution.
6.2.2
Chloride content determination
The determination of the chloride content contains several steps, such as grinding and
titration. Appendix B.1 contains an analysis of the accuracy of the titration equipment,
which is also mentioned briefly in section 4.8.3.
Grinding
The grinding of the specimens introduces two possible problems regarding determining
the correct chloride profile. Accurate measuring of grinding depth and the gathered pow­
der is not representative of the ground layer. The grinding machine has a handle to adjust
the vertical height with an accuracy of 0.1 mm. The depth was controlled with a caliper,
and it is estimated that any deviation regarding grinding depth is negligible.
There have been thoughts about whether the powder from the grinding process is repre­
sentative since a ventilation system is used for health concerns. The ventilation system
will remove the finest particles, and it is unknown if the chloride content in the finest par­
ticles is different from the rest of the particles sizes. The closed collection box shown
in fig. 4.18 was an effort to minimise this error. However, the extent of the error is not
investigated since an accurate experiment could not be derived to test this hypothesis.
Determination of acid soluble chlorides in BFC
In the development phase of the method to determine the chloride content in BFC, it came
to the author’s attention that some issues could occur when using potentiometric titration
on BFC specimens. The [ASTM C1152/C1152M­20] describes the determination of the
acid­soluble chloride content in mortar and concrete. The following is stated: ”Note 5 ­
Slags and slag cement contain sulphide sulfur in concentrations that can interfere with
chloride determination unless oxidised with hydrogen peroxide”. This is also mentioned
in [Metrohm, 2021]. Therefore, it was implemented that the BFC will follow the same
method as FAC, but with an addition of 3 ml of 30% hydrogen peroxide in the beaker after
the nitric acid was added.
The addition of hydrogen peroxide changed the solution orange. Hereafter the method
was followed as normal. The new method seemed reasonable until the determination of
the innermost samples. The innermost samples contained an allegedly negative chlo­
ride content, which is impossible. As described in appendix A.11, 1 mL of 0.1M NaCl was
56
==PAGE:64==
added extra to ensure better determination of low chloride concentration, but the endpoint
was determined to be lower than 1 mL AgNO3. After several control experiments, it was
concluded to abandon the addition of hydrogen peroxide and follow the normal proce­
dure. As a result, it estimated that the obtained chloride concentrations for BFC contain
less chlorides than stated since the measured content may be the sum of sulphide and
chlorides.
However, as long the error is systematic, will the relative difference between specimens
be the same.
6.3
Rapid chloride migration (RCM)
The results obtained by the rapid chloride migration experiment cannot directly be trans­
lated into an apparent diffusion coefficient. Therefore, comparing the two experiments is
difficult since the results are obtained under different experimental circumstances with dif­
ferent driving forces for chloride penetration. However, the development of the migration
coefficients are a direct measurement of the concrete chloride resistance. The RCM test
were cured and performed at approximately 20 °C and can not be directly compared with
the 10 °C submerged specimens.
The non­steady­state migration coefficients shown in fig. 5.33 shows that the FAC has
a slower development of resistance against chloride ingress compared to the BFC used
for this study. The figure also shows the reason why early exposure will reduce the ser­
vice life. The resistance against chlorides is significantly less at early ages, and therefore
the ingress of chloride will be more significant early. However, the low resistance is only
temporary since the resistance will be increased fast compared to the length of exposure.
The importance of this short period with low resistance is investigated in the primary in­
vestigation of this thesis.
6.3.1
Estimation of age factor
The modelling of chloride concentration with respect to time using Fick’s 2nd law is de­
scribed with the time­dependent apparent diffusion coefficient. The calculation of this
apparent diffusion coefficient contains the parameter often referred to as the ’age factor’,
α. The age factor can be quantified by making a power function fit on the data obtained
in the RCM experiment. The data from the RCM experiment is plotted double logarithmic
along with the power function fit in fig. 6.2.
The power function shows for both cases in fig. 6.2 to have a strong fit (R2 > 0.96). The
power exponent found in fig. 6.2 are reported in table 6.1 along with values reported by
fib Bulletin 34.
The studies, [Gaal, Koenders, and Polder, 2006; Attari, McNally, and Richardson, 2016],
reviews age factors and shows, in general, lower than measured by this thesis, but some
studies show age factors for BFC up to 1.2. It is difficult to compare the age factor since
significant variations in the composition of either FA or GGBFS cement.

Comparing FAC and BFC shows that BFC has greater resistance against chloride ingress
and confirms the results obtained by the rapid chloride migration test.
The effect of early age can be somewhat quantified by comparing the depth at which the
critical chloride content has been reached. For FAC­28d, this is at approximately 12 mm,
whereas FAC­2d is at approximately 19 mm, which is a 7 mm difference. For BFC, this
relation is from 6 mm to 11 mm, which is a 5 mm difference.
The effect of epoxy coating shown in fig. 6.3 is substantial and will be discussed in sec­
tion 6.6.
Within the duration of this experiment, the critical chloride content is never
reached at any point for the epoxy coated elements.
The chloride profiles shown in fig. 6.3 and 6.4 is the average values of three curves and
therefore contains a standard deviation. The best quantification of this deviation is con­
tained in the apparent diffusion coefficients and surface concentrations since these are
fitted to the whole data series. This discussion is handled in section 6.4.1.
6.4.1
Apparent diffusion coefficients and surface concentrations
The apparent diffusion coefficients determined by fitting the error solution to Fick’s 2nd
law for each obtained chloride profile is compared as the coloured bars in fig. 6.5 with the
obtained standard deviations. For direct comparison between the values, it is necessary
to consider the age of the concrete at the time of measurement. The concrete exposed
after one day is at a total age of 91 days at measurement, whereas the concrete with 28
days curing time will be 118 days. The older concrete will have a lower apparent diffusion
coefficient, and care should be taken when interpreting the values. In fig. 6.5 they grey
shaded is calculated using the equation for time­dependent apparent diffusion coefficient
(eq. (3.7)) with an age factor of 0.45 for BFC and a 0.60 for both FAC and UWC, same as
in [“fib Bulletin 34. Model code for service life design”, 2006]].
For both FAC and BFC, there is a clear tendency for a decreasing Dapp from early age
exposure to the reference exposure time at 28 days. The effect of early age exposure
increases the apparent diffusion coefficient by up to 211% for FAC compared to 28 days
curing, where it for BFC is approximate a 390% increase. It is interesting to note that the
59
==PAGE:67==
apparent diffusion coefficient for BFC­1d is lower than FAC­28d, which shows that BFC
has the best properties within the test duration with regards to chloride ingress.

As mentioned in section 6.4, the best quantification of the uncertainties is contained by
the apparent diffusion coefficient and the surface concentration. The measured chloride
profiles fit the error function solution with a very high coefficient of determination, which
on average is 0.998 for all profiles in both FAC and BFC. Therefore the apparent diffusion
coefficient can be used as a measure of uncertainties in the experiment. The standard
deviation of the apparent diffusion coefficient is shown in fig. 6.5 as error bars. When
comparing the apparent diffusion coefficients with regards to experimentally uncertain­
ties, it should be noted that the fitted values are compared, i.e. the coloured bars. The
standard deviation clearly shows that there are uncertainties involved. The relative stan­
dard deviation is between 4 % and 22%, where it for most cases is around 10 %. The
relative standard deviation is reported in [DS/EN 12390­11] as 23 %, which is more than
found by the present study.
To quantify whether there is a statistically significant difference between the obtained ap­
parent diffusion coefficient, one­way analyses of variance (ANOVA) have been conducted.
The analysis is conducted in the programming language ”R”, and the code can be seen
in appendix E. The summary of the ANOVA can be seen in fig. 6.6 where it is indicated
whether there is a significant difference (S) or not a significant difference (NS) between
all elements within each concrete type.

For both FAC and BFC, there are only a few sporadic comparisons that have a significant
difference and no clear tendency. There is no significant difference for the rest, and it
may therefore be concluded, based on the results from this experiment, that early age
exposure does not affect the surface concentration within the first 90 days of exposure.
Since there is no literature about the long­time effects of early age exposure, it is difficult
to conclude how the surface concentration changes over time when concrete is exposed
early. Therefore, it also seems appropriate to assume a constant surface concentration
and hereby minimise the variables in the modelling. Nevertheless, this assumption is most
likely invalid. For further modelling, the average value for each concrete type is therefore
used for a more convenient comparison between the specimens.
6.4.2
Chloride binding
The effect of early age exposure conditions regarding chloride binding in the exposed
concrete has been investigated by comparing the acid and water­soluble chloride profiles.
Water­soluble profiles were only determined for FAC and BFC with curing periods of 1, 7
and 28 days and can be seen in section 5.3.3.
The amount of bound chlorides is assessed as the difference between the total chloride
content obtained by the acid­soluble and the free chloride content obtained by the water­
soluble chlorides. The difference is showed in fig. 6.9 and shows clearly the relatively
small difference (even negative values) between that acid and water­soluble chloride pro­
files, which is most likely due to measurement uncertainties.
62
==PAGE:70==

-0.06

-0.02

FAC-1d

BFC-1d
BFC-7d
BFC-28d
Bound chloride content, Cbound [% dry concrete mass]
Figure 6.9: Bound chloride content obtained as the difference between acid and water­
soluble chlorides
The statistical approach to determine whether there is a difference is to perform the Stu­
dent’s t­test under the null hypothesis. Based on this test, it is confirmed that the is no sig­
nificant difference for FAC­1, FAC­7d, BFC­1d and BFC­28d. However, the test showed
that for FAC­28d and BFC­7d, there is a significant difference from the null hypothesis.
Since there is no clear tendency in the obtained results or the statistical measures, it can­
not be verified whether chloride binding is affected by early age exposure. In [Kosmatka,
Kerkhoff, and Panarese, 2005], the ratio between the acid and water­soluble chloride con­
tent is reported as about 50%. The ratio was for the Monaco Portier Cove project found
as 20­30% [Cremona et al., 2019].
The possible reason for not detecting any bound chlorides is identified either as low bind­
ing capacity, short test duration and low temperature or due to the testing method.
With regards to the low binding capacity, [Dhir, El­Mohr, and Dyer, 1996] shows that the
bounding capacity for BFC (GGBS) concrete mixes is larger than corresponding ordinary
portland cement. This is due to the increased aluminate levels in the mix, causing more
significant quantities of Friedel’s salt. The concrete mixes used contains a superplasti­
cizing additive, and it has been shown in [Haque and Kayyali, 1995] that these additives
lower the binding capacity. The experiment in the present thesis was conducted at lower
temperatures (10 °C) to simulate realistic conditions found in Denmark. The decreased
temperature will affect and decrease the chemical reaction between the chloride ions and
the aluminate phase [Yuan et al., 2009].
The testing method consists of dissolving the powder sample in demineralised water and
letting it stand 24 hours before filtration. It is by [Haque and Kayyali, 1995] claimed that
given enough time and water, all the chlorides could be recovered by the solution. The sta­
bility of Friedl’s salt was investigated in [Suryavanshi and Narayan Swamy, 1996], which
shows that it is pH dependant and that the solubility increases with a lower pH. There is
a risk of carbonation of the powder samples in the dry­grinding phase and when they are
oven­dried at 105 °C for 24 hours. The elevation of temperature increases the rate of
carbonation [Drouet et al., 2019], and since the carbonation lowers the pH, Friedl’s salt
may have been dissolved.
63
==PAGE:71==
6.5
Chloride modelling
The service life is used to estimate the effect of early age exposure by extrapolating the
results using the error function solution to Fick’s 2nd law as described in section 3.4,
eq. (3.3). Furthermore, the time dependency of the apparent diffusion is also considered
by eq. (3.7). The input values for the chloride ingress model for FAC and BFC are seen
in table 6.2.
The age factors are taken as the recommended values in [“fib Bulletin 34. Model code for
service life design”, 2006] with 0.60 for FAC and 0.45 for BFC. It has been chosen to use
these values instead of the obtained RCM age factors since the value of 0.823 for BFC
would cause the concrete never to reach a critical chloride content.
The apparent diffusion coefficients at time t0 are taken as the average value for each
concrete element presented in section 5.3.2. The time t0 is the time of exposure end, i.e.
for FAC­28d, would be 118 days.
The cover layer has been chosen as 50 mm since this is the value used in most literature
and is typical for marine structures. As described in section 6.4.1, the surface concentra­
tion is taken as constant within each concrete type. The critical chloride content is taken
as 0.1% chlorides per dry weight concrete.

The results of the modelling are shown in fig. 6.10 and 6.11 as a chloride concentration
at cover layer depth of 50 mm. Based on the chosen model parameters, it is seen that
there is a very long service life for the 28 days curing periods for both FAC and BFC (> 300
years). For the early age exposure, this service life is, as an example, 93 years for FAC­1d
and 75 years for BFC­1d. It is important to note that these service life predictions are only
based on the time to initiation of corrosion from chloride ingress and does not account for
other deterioration mechanisms, which may increase the rate of chloride ingress.

This project focuses on the effect of early age exposure, and it is therefore convenient

6.11. The shaded area shows the maximum and minimum values obtained by a sensitivity
analysis performed in the next section, section 6.5.1. The sensitivity analysis is based on
a 20 % standard deviation of the apparent diffusion coefficients and 95 % confidence
intervals. The non­linearity of the chloride ingress model causes the average values of
the intervals not to be centred in the intervals.

Figure 6.12: Relative service life (time to C = 0.1 %) with the average values of 28d
specimens for each concrete type normalised with index 1.0. The black lines indicates

minimum values obtained by the models in fig. 6.13 and 6.14 with 95 % confidence inter­
vals based on a standard deviation of apparent diffusion coefficients of 20 %. Note: The
average value is not centred in the intervals.
The reason for normalising the service life as an index within each concrete type will
be elaborated in section 6.5.1, where it is shown that of all model parameters only the
apparent diffusion coefficient and the age factor will have an effect on the relative service
life when indexing. The age factor is estimated to have a relatively small effect and is
not considered in fig. 6.12. The indexation also means that it is possible to neglect the
very long service lives shown in fig. 6.10 and 6.11, where it as an example is 900 years
of service life for BFC­28d.
With the used model, with a 20 % standard deviation and a 95 % confidence interval,
fig. 6.12 highlights the effect of early age exposure of concrete structures to marine envi­
ronments. It also shows that with chloride modelling, one should account for the uncer­
tainties when extrapolating results obtained from experiments with a short duration.
FAC shows that early age exposure (1 to 4 days) gives a significant service life reduction
between 55 % to 91 % compared to the average value of concrete exposed at 28 days.
For seven days curing period, the service life reduction is 36 % to 80 %. For 14 days
curing period, the reduction is less with ­3 % to 68 %.
BFC shows that early age exposure (1 to 4 days) gives a significant service life reduction
between 84 % to 94 % compared to the average value of concrete exposed at 28 days.
For seven days curing period, the service life reduction is 59 % to 82 %. For 14 days
curing period, the reduction is less with 22 % to 66 %.
The reduction of service life found by this study is substantially different from the values
found in the state­of­the­art report. In [Bagheri, Ajam, and Zanganeh, 2019], the effect
of service life reduction with 50 mm cover layer is 34 % for ’very early age exposure’
compared to exposure at 28 days. It should be noted that the test is conducted under
different circumstances, both in regards to exposure conditions (exposure time, temper­
66
==PAGE:74==
ature and salinity) and with regards to the service life prediction model. Therefore it is
only legitimate to compare the overall tendencies, which agrees that exposure at an early
age gives a substantial reduction of service life. The study on cementitious mortar (CEM
III/B) by [Caballero et al., 2012] showed a decrease in the service life of 10 % with a 50
mm cover layer when exposed at day one compared to day 28. Again the test conditions
and service life prediction model is different. The study of the ’Portier Cove’ in Monaco,
[Cremona et al., 2019], does not make an account of the service life reduction but con­
cludes that provisions are needed for improving durability at an early age by either 10 mm
concrete cover oversizing or a coating product.
This study and the examined studies in the state­of­the­art report shows a clear tendency.
Early age exposure of concrete to chloride ions will reduce its service life to some degree.
The studies show that the reduction can be between 10 % to 90 % when exposing after
one day of curing, but it difficult to estimate an accurate reduction.
Reduction of service life is inevitable with early age exposure of concrete and should only
be used if impractical to avoid or to reduce the construction price. It could also be possible
that the Life Cycle Costing (LCC) is reduced, but this will probably be at the expense of
the sustainability for a project.
6.5.1
Sensitivity analysis
The nature of experiments with chloride ingress is that it is subject to significant uncer­
tainties when using short­term exposures to predict the effect on a long term. Different
approaches for handling these uncertainties can be made. In the following sections, the
uncertainty for each parameter presented in table 6.2 used for the chloride ingress model
will be addressed, except the time at the end of exposure (t0), which is a fixed value.
This analysis is used to strengthen the conclusions’ validity drawn in the previous section,
section 6.5.
Apparent diffusion coefficient
The main parameters governing the chloride ingress is the apparent diffusion coefficient.
There are different approaches for handling uncertainties of this value, where for example,
the probabilistic model in [“fib Bulletin 34. Model code for service life design”, 2006] shows
a standard deviation of 20 % (they use the non­steady­state migration coefficient from
RCM). Based on this, an interval estimation is conducted, where it has been chosen to
use a 95 % confidence interval, calculated as shown in eq. (6.1).
Dapp,interval = AVG ± 1.96 · SD
√n
(6.1)
Where n is the number of samples, which for this case is 3 cylinders for each concrete
element. By calculating the maximum and minimum values for apparent diffusion coef­
ficients with above­described intervals, a spread for each model shown in fig. 6.10 and
6.11. These curves are shown in fig. 6.13 and 6.14, where it, for clarity reasons, have
been chosen to only plot two curves in each plot. The first five plots show the ’nearest
neighbour’, whereas the last five plots compare the concrete with 28 days curing time.

Age factor
The other known parameter to have the most influence on service life estimation is the
age factor. According to [“fib Bulletin 34. Model code for service life design”, 2006], the
FAC age factor should be in the interval of 0.45 to 0.75. For BFC, this interval is between
0.25 and 0.65. A way to check the sensitivity of the age factor is to plot, for example, the
chloride content at 50 mm depth after 100 years as a function of the age factor. This can
be seen in fig. 6.15, where it is both shown as the numerical value for chloride content on
the left and as an increase in chloride content if the 28 days specimens are used as index
1.0 within each concrete type.

it is instead plotted as the relative service life with 28 days specimens as index 1.0, the
difference less.

type, this would probably mean that the difference is less than 0.1.
The variation of age factor will not be included in the conclusions of this thesis, and the
recommended values of 0.45 for BFC and 0.60 for FAC will be used with no variation.

Summary
Based on the sensitivity analysis conducted, it can be concluded that the relative service
life is not affected by the choice of the cover layer, surface concentration or critical chloride
content. Therefore, it is convenient to express the effect of early age exposure as a relative
service life with regards to the 28 days cured specimens. The age factor does have an
effect if using the outermost extreme values, but it is estimated that the choice of age
factor will not have a significant influence on the conclusion drawn by this study.
However, the apparent diffusion coefficient affects the relative service life when using a
standard deviation of 20% and a confidence interval of 95%. The obtained spread is used
to conclude this study.
73
==PAGE:81==
6.6
The effect of epoxy coating
Based on the obtained chloride profiles showed in fig. 6.3, it is clear that the effect of
coating concrete with either one of the 2­component epoxy products used in this study is
substantial.
6.6.1
Application and moisture content
In the controlled laboratory environments, there were only minor issues regarding the
application of the two epoxy products on the concretes.
The first layer should be applied carefully since the excess form releasing agent on the
surface of the concrete could cause the epoxy not to have sufficient adherence. This was
especially the case for BASF MasterSeal M 338 and is probably due to the product being
diluted with 10 % water instead of 5 % as for SIKA Sikagard®­Wallcoat N. It could also
be a coincidence that there was more excess form oil on some of the concrete elements.
When applying the two epoxy products, it were also noticed that it could be difficult to
block the surface pores and the pore structure would absorb the epoxy. This was also
observed when grinding the concrete powder for the chloride profile. For all epoxy coated
products, it was found that it was necessary to remove 0.2 mm. On the left of fig. 6.20 it
is possible to see that a small amount of epoxy is still present in the pores after 0.2 mm
grinding.
Figure 6.20: Left: Epoxy coated cylinder (BASF) after 0.2 mm is removed. Center: Epoxy
cotaed cylinder (SIKA). Right: Concrete element with drilled cylinders (SIKA).
All four concrete elements, which were to be epoxy coated, were measured beforehand
with a calibrated NOVANEX surface moisture indicator. For both one day and curing pe­
riods (time of application), the moisture indicator showed above 10% water content (max.
value for the instrument). SIKA reports that Sikagard®­Wallcoat N should be applied at
≤6% (by weight) moisture content. BASF reports that MasterSeal M 338 should not be
applied for a value higher than 8%.
An additional study has been conducted to determine the water content of the concrete at 1
and 21 maturity days and is presented in appendix B.2. For each of the two curing periods,
a Ø100x200 mm cylinder was cast, demoulded after 24 hours and plastic wrapped for the
remaining curing period. The approach is identical to the concrete elements, and the only
difference are the size which will cause a small difference in hydration temperatures. After
74
==PAGE:82==
the curing period, the cylinders are crushed and oven­dried at 105 °C until constant mass
is achieved. The results of the additional study is presented in table 6.3.
Table 6.3: Moisture content of a FAC after 1 and 21 days.
1 day
21 days
Moisture content, u [%]
5.63
4.03
The results show that the measured moisture contents for both cases is below the surface
value limits given by BASF and SIKA, which contradicts the water contents obtained by
the surface moisture indicator.
6.6.2
Modelling
There is no statistically significant difference between the use of SIKA Sikagard®­Wallcoat
N and BASF MasterSeal M 338 in this study. Therefore, for further discussion, there will
not be distinguished between the two products and will now be referred to as ”epoxy
coating”.
When extrapolating the results obtained by this study to determine the service life based
on a critical chloride content, the earlier proposed model of Fick’s 2nd law of diffusion is
no longer applicable due to the inhomogeneity presented by a two­layer system.
The standard, [NT BUILD 489: Concrete, hardened, repair: Chloride penetration resis­
tance of paint on concrete, 1999], presents a measuring principle based on compara­
tive measurements of the chloride ingress into uncoated references as wall as coated
elements. The apparent diffusion coefficient and the boundary conditions of the chlo­
ride profile are calculated for the uncoated reference elements. The distances (in­depth)
through which the measured chloride profile in the painted elements must be moved to
obtain the best fit to the chloride profile in the reference elements is named the ”equivalent
concrete thickness” of the coating system. The method is constructed for an accelerated
laboratory exposure to a sodium chloride solution, but the principle can be transferred to
the case of this study.
For this study, the reference elements would be FAC­2d and FAC­28d, whereas the corre­
sponding epoxy coated elements would be FAC­2d­SIKA/BASF and FAC­28d­SIKA/BASF.
The concept of stating the effect of the epoxy as an equivalent concrete thickness, L’, is
shown in fig. 6.21. The equivalent concrete thickness has been found by minimising the
error between the two curves.
When using the equivalent concrete thickness for further analysis to determine the service
life, Cranks solution to Fick’s 2nd law will be used with the apparent diffusion coefficient
and surface concentration obtained from the reference elements.
Cepoxy(x, t) = Cs,ref −(Cs,ref −Ci) ·
[
1 −erf
(
L′ + x
2
√
Dapp,ref(t) · t
)]
(6.2)
This model is calculated for both FAC with 2 and 28 days curing time before exposure with
an identical and constant surface concentration, initial chloride content and age factor for
comparison. The results are shown in fig. 6.22 with the change of concentrations with
respect to time at a constant depth of 50 mm.
75

Figure 6.22: Estimating the effect of epoxy coating by using the equivalent concrete thick­
ness as proposed by [NT BUILD 489]. The chloride concentrations are shown at a con­
crete depth of 50 mm. The critical chloride content have been chosen as 0.1 % concrete
weight.
Figure 6.22 shows that the epoxy coating products used for this study with the chosen
model parameters will give an elongation of service life with a factor of 5.4 for a 2 day
cured element, whereas the effect will be 3.6 times for the 28 days cured element. These
values will, however, primarily be affected by the apparent diffusion coefficient and the
age factor as presented in sensitivity analysis in section 6.5. Therefore, the reported
elongation of service life cannot be used for general purposes, but they show a tendency
of epoxy coating as a service life enhancer.
The presented model with equivalent concrete thickness addition does not account for
76
==PAGE:84==
degradation of the coating product, and the extrapolation of the results should therefore
be taken with care. The 90 days of exposure did not show any visual signs of degradation.
The results obtained are also based on a relatively short exposure period and could be
different if a more extended period has been investigated. It can be concluded that the
epoxy coating is effective for at least 90 days, which thereby extend the period of the
first real contact with seawater beyond that and eliminate the negative effect of early age
exposure.
Based on the results presented in this study, there is no doubt that the effect of the epoxy
coating products is substantial and that they can be used to expose concrete for marine
environments at an early age. However, it should be noted that the concrete has been
coated in a controlled laboratory environment, and the careful procedures used for this
study may not be followed on a construction site. The moisture content is also determined
in a controlled dry environment and does not account for weathering actions.
6.7
Underwater concrete (UWC)
This study also contains the ’extreme’ of early age exposure, where concrete is cast di­
rectly underwater using the tremie method. Several factors are essential for a successful
UWC execution, such as mix design, workability, flow retention, stability and early age
strength gain [Guide to Tremie Concrete for Deep Foundations, 2018]. It was therefore
chosen to order a well known and tested recipe from Unicon A/S. On the day of casting,
everything went as expected. Regarding the fresh properties, the slump flow was 600
mm and the visual stability index of 0 (concrete mass is homogeneous and no evidence
of bleeding).
Examples provided by [Guide to Tremie Concrete for Deep Foundations, 2018] shows
that the flow pattern of concrete is difficult to predict, but the guide shows a cross­section
where it is visually possible to see the flow patterns. The following fig. 6.23 and fig. 6.24 is
an example of the visual chloride front of UWC­0d (bedplate) and UWC­1d (wall element).
The large split in the middle of UWC­0d is due to the element being too large to handle
for the water­cooled diamond saw, and it was necessary to saw from each side.
TOP
TOP
Figure 6.23: Cross section of UWC­0d (bedplate) as shown in the figure on the right. The
cross section is sprayed with 0.1 M AgNO3.
For the bedplate it has been assessed that there is few to none aggregates in the upper
approximately 20 mm (sludge layer). It is therefore questionable whether the application
of Fick’s 2nd law is valid with two different layer properties.

Figure 6.24: Cross section of UWC­1d (wall element) as shown in the figure on the right.

By a visual assessment of the chloride front, which for all cases are approximately con­
stant, it is assessed that the casting process is of satisfactory quality (neglecting the ex­
pected sludge layer).
The chloride profiles obtained by UWC seen in fig. 6.25 highlights possible durability prob­
lems when casting a bedplate subjected to chloride ingress from the moment of casting.
The critical chloride content is reached at approximately 23.5 mm, which is an increase
of about 9 mm compared to the wall elements. As a comparison UWC­1d reaches the
critical chloride content at 14 mm. FAC­1d reaches the critical chloride content at around
18 mm.
With regards to the bedplate, it should, however, be noted that when casting with the
tremie method, the upper layer will always be sludge which is included in the shown chlo­
ride profile. Due to the sludge layer surface variation, it was difficult to determine a zero
point for the grinding intervals. It was chosen to use the ’first contact’ with the dry­grinder
as the zero point. However, the relative standard deviation of the apparent diffusion co­
efficient is 9 %, which compared to UWC­7d (10 %) is less. It could be argued that the
chloride profile should be shifted to represent the concrete with structural integrity. This
was not accessed practically possible, and the ’raw’ results are therefore presented.

Figure 6.26 shows the estimated service life with a cover layer of 50 mm. The estimated
for UWC­0d, UWC­1d, UWC­1d­REF and UWC 7d service life, respectively, is 6 years,

noted that the sludge layer is included in this analysis and that Fick’s 2nd law is probably
not valid with two different layer properties.
The ANOVA analysis conducted previously in fig. 6.6c and fig. 6.8c showed that there
was no significant difference for neither the apparent diffusion coefficient or surface con­
centration between UWC­1d, UWC­1­REF and UWC­7d. It is, therefore, based on the
knowledge of the sensitivity analysis conducted for FAC and BFC in section 6.5.1, ques­
tionable whether the significant differences in service life is valid. Based on the ANOVA it
can also be concluded that there is no significant difference between tremie method and
the reference casting method used in this study.
79
==PAGE:87==
7
Conclusion
This study shows that early age exposure results in a significant decrease in service life
compared to normal curing time unless protective measures in the form of increasing
cover layer or efficient coating are applied.
The development of the chloride migration coefficients was investigated by a rapid chloride
migration (RCM) test at different ages between 2 and 92 days for FAC and BFC. The study
shows the following:
• There is a rapid increase of chloride resistance for both FAC and BFC within the first
18 days.
• The chloride migration coefficients for BFC is lower than FAC.
• The obtained development of chloride migration coefficients have a strong fit to the
power function.
• The obtained age factors are higher than some studies reports and is therefore not
used for further modelling.
A diffusion test was performed by submerging elements in 10 °C seawater from Oresund,
Denmark. As a result of this, the chloride profiles were determined by potentiometric
titration. The apparent diffusion coefficients and surface concentrations were calculated
by fitting the chloride profiles to the solution to Fick’s 2nd law. From the results obtained
by the diffusion test, the following can be concluded:
• The study shows that the concrete with CEM III/B (BFC) have a greater resistance
to chloride ingress than CEM II/A­V (FAC) within the 90 days exposure to seawater.
• The apparent diffusion coefficients for BFC­1d is lower than for FAC­28d.
• Analysis of variance shows a significant statistical difference between early age ex­
posure and exposure after 28 days of curing for both FAC and BFC.
For assessment of the long time durability regarding chloride ingress, a simplified diffusion
model was used. The service life modelling regarding chloride ingress for the different cur­
ing times compared to the normal curing time of 28 days. The following can be concluded
based on the modelling:
• There are large uncertainties when extrapolating results, which affects the accuracy
of the conclusion.
• FAC shows that early age exposure (1 to 4 days) gives a significant service life
reduction between 55 % and 91 % compared to the average value of concrete ex­
posed at 28 days. For a 7 days curing period, the service life reduction is between
36 % and 80%. For 14 days curing period, the reduction is between ­3 % and 68%.
• BFC shows that early age exposure (1 to 4 days) gives a significant service life
reduction between 84 % and 94 % compared to the average value of concrete ex­
posed at 28 days. For a 7 days curing period, the service life reduction is between
59 % and 82%. For 14 days curing period, the reduction is between 22% to 66%.
• Exposure to chlorides at an early age will significantly reduce the service life.
80
==PAGE:88==
It was also investigated if this reduction in service life could be minimised using a epoxy
coating. Two types of epoxy coatings were used. For each epoxy coating, the following
two elements were coated. One element was coated after one day of curing and then
exposed after one day of additional curing. The second element was coated after 21
days of curing and then exposed after 7 days of additional curing time (28 days). The
following can be concluded:
• The use of epoxy coating significantly reduces the chloride ingress compared to the
reference curing time of 28 days without coating, thus increasing service life.
• The coating has an effect equivalent to 20 mm and 15 mm of extra cover layer for
respectively for 2 and 28 days curing, using the method described in [NT BUILD
489].
• It is possible to apply epoxy at an early age and have sufficient resistance for at
least 90 days.
• Epoxy coatings can be used to avoid the effect of early age exposure and still in­
crease the service life compared to the normal curing time of 28 days.
Investigation of underwater concrete was also performed. The concrete was cast directly
in moulds with 10 °C seawater using the tremie method. Three types of elements were
investigated: A bedplate which is exposed immediately after casting to chlorides. Two
wall elements with moulds removed after respectively 1 and 7 days, thus exposing the
concrete to chlorides. The last element was a control element cast in a dry mould and
exposed after 1 day of curing. This investigation shows the following:
• There is a significant chloride ingress and possible durability problems regarding
chloride ingress when casting underwater concrete as a bedplate.
• There is no statistically significant difference in apparent diffusion coefficients be­
tween casting directly in seawater and dry mould.
• There is no significant difference between the apparent diffusion coefficients ob­
tained for the two wall elements exposed at either 1 or 7 days.
• There is a need for more research on chloride ingress in underwater cast bedplate
structures.
81
==PAGE:89==
8
Further work
The present study, along with the existing literature within the subject, agrees on the
tendency of service life reduction for concrete structures exposed to chloride ions at early
ages. However, the effect varies by several factors, which highlights the problem within
chloride ingress; it is case­specific and should be experimentally tested for each new
case.
It is common for all presented studies and the present thesis that the effect of early age
exposure is measured after a relatively short duration of exposure (< 1 year). Chloride
ingress models with inadequate precision then extrapolate the results obtained by the
short duration experiments. Therefore, it could be interesting to conduct the series of
experiments presented in the present study, but with an increased exposure duration of
several years or decades. To generalise the results, it is of utmost importance to test the
effect of both temperature and salinity of the exposure condition and different concrete
types. It is the hope that these long term experiments would help in the development of
a precise chloride ingress model for concrete exposed at early ages.
There is no public research on the durability of underwater cast concrete (or tremie con­
crete) to the authors’ knowledge. The hope with this thesis is that the durability problems
regarding chloride ingress of underwater cast concrete are highlighted, and further re­
search on the topic will be conducted.

A.1
Sieving method for aggregates
The particle size distribution should comply with the Fuller curve in [Munch­Petersen,
2013] and is determined by dry sieving according to the standard [DS/EN 933­1: Test for
geometrical properties of aggregates ­ Part 1: Determination of particle size distribution ­
Sieving method, 2013].
Equipment
• Test sieves, with apparatus specified in the standard [DS/EN 933­2: Tests for geo­
metrical properties of aggregates – Part 2: Determination of particle size distribution
– Test sieves, nominal size of apertures, 2020].
• Mechanical sieving machine
• Tightly fitting pan and lid for the sieves
• Ventilated oven capable of maintaining 110 ◦C ± 5 ◦C
• Scale
• Trays
• Brushes
Materials
• Samples of all aggregate types used in section 4.2, with a minimum mass of test
portions according to the standard [DS/EN 933­1: Test for geometrical properties
of aggregates ­ Part 1: Determination of particle size distribution ­ Sieving method,
2013].
Procedure
1. Samples are prepared by the ’sample reduction by quartering method’ in accordance
to the standard [DS/EN 932­2: Test for general properties of aggregates ­ Part 2:
Methods for reducing laboratory samples, 1999].
2. The samples are dried in the ventilated oven at a temperature of 110◦C ±5◦C until
it reaches a constant mass.
3. Weigh the whole sample and note it down as M1
4. Weigh the individual empty sieves and note it down as m0
5. The sieving column is fitted together and arranged, from top to bottom, in order of
decreasing sieve size.
6. The dried sample is poured in the sieving column
7. The sieving column is shaken mechanically at 50 Hz for 30 minutes.
8. Weigh the individual sieves with retained samples and note it down as m1
89
==PAGE:97==
Calculation of Fines Passing
The percentage of fines passing each sieve is calculated from eq. (A.1)
f = m1 −m0
M1
· 100%
(A.1)
Where
f
is the percentage of fines passing each sieve, in %
M1
is the dried mass of the test portion, in kg.
m0
is the mass of the empty sieve, in kg.
m1
is the retained mass in the sieve, in kg.

==PAGE:98==
A.2
Moisture content in aggregates
Prior to the mixing process of concrete, the mix design should be adjusted to take account
of the moisture content in the aggregates. The moisture content is determined by drying
the aggregates using a microwave oven until constant mass is achieved.
Equipment
• Microwave oven at 900 W
• Scale
• Heat resistant glass container
Materials
• Representative samples of all aggregate types used in section 4.2.
Procedure
1. Weigh the heat resistant containers and note the weight as mc
2. The representative sample of relevant aggregates are filled in the containers. Note
that the moisture content in storage can vary from top to bottom.
3. Weigh the container with aggregates and note the weight as m
4. Insert the filled container into the the microwave oven and turn it on for 10 min­
utes. Note: It is important to insert a glass bottle with demineralised water in the
microwave oven in order to protect is from burning down.
5. After 10 minutes, the container is removed from the microwave oven and the weight
is noted.
6. This procedure is repeated in 5 minutes intervals until a constant mass is achieved.
This is noted as the dry weight, m0.
7. Calculate the moisture content from eq. (A.2)
8. The moisture content is used in section 4.2 to adjust the mix design.
Calculation of Moisture Content
The moisture content is calculated from eq. (A.2).
u = m −m0
m0 −mc
· 100%
(A.2)
Where
u
is the moisture content, in %
m
is the mass of the aggregates and container before drying, in kg.
m0
is the mass of the aggregates and container after drying, in kg
mc
is the mass of the heat resistant container, in kg
91
==PAGE:99==
A.3
Mixing and casting of FAC and BFC
The approach of producing the concrete elements and cylinders for FAC and BFC is de­
scribed here, which involves preparation, mixing, pouring, vibration and curing.
Equipment
• Moulds for elements (phenolic film faced plywood for concrete elements, dimension:
150x220x460 mm)
• Moulds for cylinders (standard Ø100x200 mm cylinders conforming with [DS/EN
12390­1: Testing hardened concrete ­ Part 1: Shape, dimensions and other re­
quirements for specimens and moulds, 2019])
• Vibration table (50 Hz)
• Buckets
• Weights
• Concrete mixer, rotating pan mixer (Zyklos ZZ 50 HE), see fig. A.1
• Scoop
• Straightening stick
Materials
• Sand (Storebæltsand, 0­2 mm)
• Granite (Blå Rønne Granit, 4­8 mm)
• Granit (Blå Rønne Granit, 11­16)
• Cement (CEM II/A­V for FAC or CEM III/B for BFC)
• Superplasticizer (Mapai Dynamon XTend 200
• Form release agent (Mapai Mapeform Eco Oil 700)
• Tap water
• Plastic cover
• Duct tape
Figure A.1: Zyklos ZZ 50 HE rotating pan mixer
92
==PAGE:100==
Preparation
1. The moulds which are to be used (element and cylinders) placed on the vibration
table
2. Apply form releasre agent on the clean, dry formwork, preferably in a single coat,
using pressure pumps fitted with a misting nozzle and wiping it with clean paper.
3. Prepare equipment and materials for determination of slump measurement, air con­
tent and density according to appendix A.4, A.6 and A.7.
4. Prepare the concrete mixer
Mixing
1. Determine the water content in the aggregates (see appendix A.2) and correct the
mix design as described in section 4.2.
2. Weigh the aggregates, cement and water in individual buckets.
3. Weigh the superplasticizer in a suitable container.
4. Add all the granite and half of the sand to the mixer.
5. Add the cement followed by the rest of the sand.
6. Dry­mix the constituents for 30 seconds.
7. While the mixing continuous the water is added.
8. Directly after, the superplasticizer is added slowly.
9. The the mixing continue for approximately 3 minutes.
10. After mixing, determination of slump measurement, air content and density is per­
formed.
Pouring and vibration
1. Using a scoop, the mixed fresh concrete is poured in the prepared moulds, which
are only filled half.
2. The vibration table is turned on at 50 Hz. Vibration continues until just before water
appears at the surface.
3. Fill up the moulds completely and vibrated the same way.
4. Remove excess concrete from the top by using a is stricken off with a straightening
stick using a sawing motion.
5. The concrete element mould is closed with a plastic cover and held down by metal
plates
6. The cylinders moulds are closed with the associated lids which then is fastened by
a clamp.
Demoulding and curing of concrete elements
1. The concrete elements is placed in its mould for 24 hours in a temperature controlled
curing room with 20°C.
93
==PAGE:101==
2. After 24 hours, the concrete element is demoulded and wrapped in plastic cover,
which it tightly secured by duct tape. Note: the element which should only cure for
24 hours before seawater exposure is not plastic wrapped after demoulding.
3. The curing continues in the temperature controlled room until the desired curing age
is reached (1, 2, 4, 7, 14 or 28 days).
4. Remove the plastic wrapping
5. Place the concrete element is the seawater tanks, which are temperature controlled
at 10°C for 90 days.
Demoulding and curing of cylinders
1. The cylinders is placed in the mould for 24 hours in a temperature controlled curing
room with 20°C.
2. After 24 hours, the cylinders are demoulded
3. Place the cylinders in curing tank filled with tap water at 20°C for 28 days or the
desired curing age for RCM cylinders.

==PAGE:102==
A.4
Slump measurement of fresh concrete
The slump measurement test of the fresh concrete is determined according to the stan­
dard [DS/EN 12350­2: Testing fresh concrete ­ Part 2: Slump test, 2019].
Equipment
• Hollow cone (h = 300 mm, dtop = 100 mm, dbottom = 200 mm)
• Compacting rod
• Funnel
• Straightening stick
• Ruler
• Base plate
• Moist cloth
• Scoop
Procedure
1. The sample of fresh concrete is obtained according to appendix A.3.
2. The cone and base plate are moistened by the cloth. The cone is placed on the
base plate and fastened using the clamps.
3. Fill the cone in three layers, each approximately one­third of the cone height.
4. Each layer is compacted with 25 strokes of the compacting rod. The base plate
may not be hit. The strokes should be uniformly distributed over the cross section
of each layer, ie. the strokes must only just penetrate into the underlying layer.
5. If the compacting of the top layer results in subsidence below top edge, ad more
concrete to keep and excess above the top of the cone at all times.
6. After compacting top layer, strike off the concrete with a straightening stick with a
sawing motion.
7. Remove spilled concrete from base plate.
8. Hold the cone fixed while loosening the clamps.
9. Remove the cone from the concrete by raising it carefully in a vertical direction. This
operation should take 2 to 5 seconds, by a steady upward lift.
10. Flip the cone upside down and measure the difference from the cone height to the
highest point of the slumped test specimen.
95
==PAGE:103==
A.5
Slump flow measurement of fresh concrete
The slump flow measurement of the UWC is determined according to the standard [DS/EN
12350­8: Testing fresh concrete ­ Part 8: Self­compacting concrete ­ Slump­flow test,
2019]. The baseplate material deviates from the standard, which uses a steel plate.
Furthermore the visual stability index is reported according to [ASTM C1611: Standard
Test Method for Slump Flow of Self­Consolidating Concrete, 2018].
Equipment
• Baseplate, as shown in fig. A.2
• Hollow cone (h = 300 mm, dtop = 100
mm, dbottom = 200 mm)
• Spirit level
• Ruler
• Trowel
• Moist cloth
Figure A.2: Equipment for slump flow mea­
surement
Procedure
1. Place the baseplate on a flat and horizontal surface and adjust with a spririt level.
2. The baseplate and hollow cone is dampened with the moist cloth.
3. Place the hollow cone in the centre of the baseplate upside­down, as shown in
fig. A.2.
4. Fill the cone in one operation without any agitation of mechanical compaction and
strike of the surplus from the of the cone with a trowel.
5. Lift the cone vertical in 1 to 3 seconds in one movement without interfering with the
flow of concrete.
6. After the flow of concrete has stabilised, measure the largest diameter of the flow
spread and record it as d1 to the nearest 10 mm. Then measure the diameter of the
flow spread at right angles to d1 to the nearest 10 mm. If the difference between d1
and d2 is greater than 50 mm, another sample shall be taken.
7. Check the concrete spread for signs of segregation and report in a qualitative way.
Expression of results
The slump­flow, SF, is the mean of d1 and d2, expressed to the nearest 10 mm and
calculated as eq. (A.3)
SF = d1 + d2
2
(A.3)
Where
96
==PAGE:104==
SF
is slump­flow, in mm
d1
is the largest diameter of flow spread, in mm
d2
is the flow spread at 90◦to d1, in mm
97
==PAGE:105==
A.6
Air content of fresh concrete
The air content of the fresh concrete is determined according to the standard [DS/EN
12350­7: Testing fresh concrete ­ Part 7: Air content ­ Pressure methods, 2019].
Equipment
• Pressure meter (8L), see fig. A.3
• Vibration table
• Demineralised water in spray tube
• Straightening stick
Figure A.3: Air content testing device (pressure meter)
Procedure
1. The sample (8L) of fresh concrete is obtained according to appendix A.3.
2. The air test container is cleaned and moistened with demineralised water prior to
starting the test.
3. The air test container is placed in the vibration table.
4. Using the scoop, the concrete is filled in half of the pressure meter without entrapping
air.
5. The vibration table is turned on at 50 Hz. Vibration continues until just before water
appears at the surface.
6. The air container is now completely filled with concrete and vibrated in the same
way.
7. After concrete has been compacted, the top of the air container is stricken off with
the straightening stick using a sawing motion.
8. The flanges of the air test container is thoroughly cleaned and assembled, in such
a way that it is pressure tight.
9. Water is injected trough the appropriate valve until water emerges from the opposite
valve. Both valves are closed simultaneously.
10. The pressure meter is calibrated with the hand pump (ie.
pump until pressure
reaches 0).
11. The measuring is done by pressing the black button and read off the air content from
the pressure gauge.
98
==PAGE:106==
A.7
Concrete density of fresh concrete
The density of the fresh concrete is determined according to the standard [DS/EN 12350­
6: Testing fresh concrete ­ Part 6: Density, 2019]. The experiment deviates from the
standard regarding the density container used. For simplicity, the air test container used
in appendix A.6 is used. The pressure meter holds 8 L of fresh concrete.
Equipment
• Pressure meter from appendix A.6
• Weight
Procedure
1. Before filling the pressure meter, the initial weight is noted as m1 and to the nearest
0.01 kg.
2. The pressure meter is filled and compacted according to appendix A.6.
3. Weigh the pressure meter with its content to determine the mass m2 to the nearest
0.01 kg and note it.
Calculation of Density
The density is calculated from eq. (A.4).
ρ = m2 −m1
V
(A.4)
Where
ρ
is the density of the fresh concrete, in kg/m3
m1
is the mass of the empty pressure container, in kg.
m2
is the mass of the pressure container filled with compacted concrete, in kg
V
is the volume of the pressure container (8L), in m3

A.8
Epoxy application
The method of coating the relevant concrete elements with 2­component epoxy products
(SIKA Sikagard®­Wallcoat N and BASF MasterSeal M 338) is described in detail in this
appendix. The product datasheets are available in appendix F.3 and F.4.
Both epoxy products will have a layer thickness for the first layer is 200 g/m2, whereas it is
250 g/m2 for the second layer. The first layer of BASF MasterSeal M338 are to be diluted
with 10% water, whereas the SIKA Sikagaard­Wallcoat N are to be diluted with 5% water
according to the datasheets. Both products are a 2­component epoxy and are therefore
to be mixed as stated by the product datasheet at the given ratio between component A
and component B.
Equipment
• Lab coat
• Safety glasses
• Plastic gloves
• Stirrers (2 large and 1 small)
• Plastic foil
• Plastic bag
• 2 scoop spoons
• Fume hood
• Plastic container
• Scale
• Brushes
• Supports
Materials
• Concrete elements (FAC) at age 1 and 21 days as described in appendix A.3
• SIKA Sikagard®­Wallcoat N (component A and B)
• BASF MasterSeal M 338 (component A and B)
Procedure
1. Check data­sheets for relevant information
2. Calculate the desired amount for each side of the concrete element corresponding
to current layer.
3. Prepare all equipment and materials and place them in the fume hood
4. Prepare a plastic back for disposing the epoxy trash
5. Place the concrete element on the supports in the fume hood. The side that is
facing upwards is painted. When painting other sides, the concrete element should
be turned so the surface pointing upwards is painted.
6. Put on safety equipment
100
==PAGE:108==
7. Stir epoxy component A and B in the respective containers for 3 minutes.
8. Place the empty mixing plastic container in the scale
9. Weigh the desired amount of component A in the plastic container
10. Weigh the desired amount of component B in the plastic container
11. Only for first layer: SIKA Sikagard®­Wallcoat N is diluted with 5% tap water. BASF
MasterSeal M 338 is diluted with 10% tap water.
12. Mix the two components with stirrer for 3 minutes.
13. Weigh the mixed epoxy along with the brush and note the weight.
14. Apply an even layer of epoxy to the upwards facing surface until the mass corre­
sponding to the current layer.
15. Rotate the element and repeat the last 2 steps for the remaining sides.
16. When the first layer of epoxy is dry, repeat the last 3 steps for the second layer with
the new layer thickness (recalculate the desired amount for each side).
17. Dispose all equipment used connected to the mixing and application process for
chemical waste.
101
==PAGE:109==
A.9
Compressive strength of hardened concrete
The compressive strength of the hardened concrete is determined according to the stan­
dard [DS/EN 12390­3: Testing hardened concrete ­ Part 3: Compressive strength ­ Spec­
ification for testing machines, 2019].
Equipment
• Compression testing machine, TONY 3000 (conforming to EN 12390­4:2019), as
shown in fig. A.4
• Safety shield
• Cast cylinders (Ø100x200 mm)
Figure A.4: TONY 3000 compression testing machine
Procedure
1. Cast cylinders (Ø100x200 mm) (according to appendix A.3 or ??) are removed from
curing tank after 28 maturity days and tested as soon as practicable.
2. All bearing surfaces of the testing machine is wiped
3. Wipe excess moisture from the surface of the specimen before placing in the testing
machine.
4. Place the cylinder vertically and centre it with respect to the lower pressure plate.
5. Turn on the testing machine.
6. Press ”START” to enter the menu. ”PARM” for parameters.
7. Navigate in the menu with the arrow button and choose the following:
(a) Test type: ”Compress”
(b) Contol: ”F=LOAD”
(c) Disp: ”F [kN]”
8. Press ”START” to continue.
9. Set the dimensions of the specimen to ”d[mm]=100.00” and ”h[mm]=200.00”.
10. Set dF/dt to 4.712 kN/s corresponding to a speed of 0.6 ± 0.2 MPa/s according to
EN 12390­3.
102
==PAGE:110==
11. Set ”FRACT.DET: LOAD [%]” to 0.150 as an initial setting. The machine will then
continue the test.
12. Press ”START” to confirm and the display will show the entered settings. Press
”START” to confirm.
13. The pressure plates will adjust according to the cylinder. Press ”START” to begin
the test.
14. The machine stops when failure occurs and the failure load is displayed. This value
should be noted and 7 kN is subtracted, which is a correcting value based on a
calibration of the machine.
15. Press ”START” and the machine lowers the piston. The safety shield and cylinder
is removed.
16. Clean the pressure plates and repeat test with the other cylinders.
17. Press ”STOP” after last test. The machine lowers the piston completely.
18. Press ”STOP” once more and the machine turns off.
19. Remove the last tested cylinder and clean the pressure plates.
20. Turn off the testing machine.
21. The cylinders are assessed with regards to their failure type. Satisfactory failure
types are shown in fig. A.5 and are according to EN 12390­3:2019.
22. The compressive strength is calculated according to eq. (A.5).
Figure A.5: Satisfactory failure of cylinder specimen according to EN 12390­3:2019
Calculation of Compressive Strength
The compressive strength is calculated from eq. (A.5).
fc = F
Ac

Where
fc
is the compressive strength, in MPa
F
is the failure load, in N
Ac
is the cross sectional area of the specimen, in mm2

A.10
Drilling and grinding of hardened concrete specimens
The cores are first drilled from the concrete elements and powder is obtained at certain
depths by dry­grinding. The principle of profile grinding of concrete core is according to
[DS/EN 12390­11: Testing hardened concrete ­ Part 11: Determination of the chloride
resistance of concrete, unidirectional diffusion (technical specification), 2019].
Equipment
• Water cooled diamond drill (Ø100 mm)
• Grinding machine with a diamond grinder wheel capable of running without water
cooling and adjustable in height with 0.1 mm precision, as shown in fig. A.7
• Powder collection box with lid, as shown in fig. A.8
• Air compressor
• Zip plastic bags capable of holding at least 20 g of concrete powder
Procedure
1. The concrete element is removed from seawater exposure and marked with a paint
marker where the 3 cores are to be drilled.
2. The concrete element is placed accordingly in the water cooled diamond drill and
the 3 cores are extracted, as shown in fig. A.6.
3. The cores are transported to the grinding machine.
4. Based on expected chloride ingress, the 9 grinding intervals is determined. Each
interval is written on a zip plastic bag along with the core identification number.
5. A concrete core is inserted in the grinding machine and secured tightly in the bench
vice, as shown in fig. A.7. Rubber strips are used between the core and the clamp
to ensure a better fit. Make sure that the core is completely flush with bottom of the
vice.
6. The collection box, as shown in fig. A.8, is inserted around the concrete core and the
position of the box is adjusted with another clamp. The position of the box should be
so that the top of the core is approximately centred with respect to the box height.
7. The position of the core is adjusted until the diamond grinder wheel is exactly on top
of the core. This level is calibrated to zero at the equipment level indicator.
8. The level of the core is adjusted according to the desired interval.
9. The lid is mounted on the powder collecting box, as shown in fig. A.9.
10. The green button is pressed to start the diamond grinder wheel and the black lever
is pulled and the core will start moving towards the diamond grinder wheel. When
the diamond grinder wheel has ground the whole surface, the red stop button is
pressed and the black lever is pulled back in original position.
11. The position of core is manually controlled until it is possible to remove the collection
box.
12. The collection box is removed and collected in the zip plastic bag with corresponding
identification.
104
==PAGE:112==
13. After each interval the diamond grinder wheel, plastic collection box (and lid) is thor­
oughly cleaned with a compressor.
14. Repeat step 8­13 for each interval.
Figure A.6: Core drilling
Figure A.7: Grinding machine
Figure A.8: Powder collection box
Figure A.9: Powder collection box with lid
105

A.11
Chloride profile by potentiometric titration (acid and
water soluble)
Present appendix describes the procedure for determining both the acid and water sol­
uble chlorides in the samples collected by profile grinding (appendix A.10). The titration
procedure is identical, however the sample preparation differs.
The method used to determine the acid soluble chloride content is based on [DS/EN
14629: Products and systems for the protection and repair of concrete structures ­ Test
methods ­ Determination of chloride content in hardened concrete, 2007]. Method B (Po­
tentiometric titration) is used since it is a more precise (For the authors) and automatic
method than method A (Volhard titration). For reference, the corresponding American
standard is [ASTM C1152/C1152M­20: Standard Test Method for Acid­Soluble Chloride
in Mortar and Concrete. 2020].
The method used to determine the water soluble chloride content in the samples is based
on the American standard [ASTM C1218: Standard Test Method for Water­Soluble Chlo­
ride in Mortar and Concrete, 2020].
Equipment
Chemicals
• Nitric acid: HNO3, concentratedd
• Nitric acid: HNO3, 1% solution
• Silver nitrate: AgNO3, 0.1 M solution
• Sodium chloride: NaCl, 0.1 M solution
• Demineralised water
Equipment
• Thermostatically controlled oven, 105±5◦C
• Balance with accuracy ±0.01 g or better
• Glass bottles, holding approx. 300 mL
• Funnel
• Filter paper
• Kettle for boiling demineralised water
• Automatic titrator with Ag/AgCl electrode (888 Titrando)
Sample
• Concrete powder samples for each layer analysed. Powder sample is obtained as
described by appendix A.10.
Sample preparation (acid soluble chlorides)
A large proportion of the chloride in concrete is bound in hydrates. To determine the total
chloride content, the chloride need to be extracted by acid.
1. Weigh the glass bottle (1)
2. Place approx. 5 g of sample in the glass bottle (1)
106
==PAGE:114==
3. Dry the powder sample in the 105±5°C oven until constant mass
4. Register the mass of the dry sample
5. Add 20 mL demineralized water and shake the bottle to achieve separation of the
particles
6. Add 10 mL concentrated nitric acid, as shown in fig. A.10, and shake the bottle
7. Add 50 mL hot demineralized water and shake the bottle again
8. Let the mixture cool
9. *Add 1 mL concentrated nitric acid. If the gas evolution is observed (the solution
makes a fizz), add more acid
10. Filter the solution in glass bottle (1) into the glass bottle (2), as shown in fig. A.11.
Rinse the filter with 1% nitric acid
11. Add demineralised water to a total volume of 200 mL
12. **Add 1.00 mL 0.1 M sodium chloride solution
The sample preparation is essentially identical with the sample preparation described in
[DS/EN 14629], with the following expectations:
*
step 9 is not part of DS/EN 14629. However, the chloride is only going to be re­
leased from the hydrates if the solution is acidic. If the sample has a high calcium
hydroxide content (Ca(OH)2), then 10 mL concentrated nitric acid is not enough to
change the initial high pH value of the sample. The extra addition of concentrated
nitric action is to check the condition of the solution: If gas is released when acid is
added, this indicates that the acid is reacting with a base (calcium hydroxide), and
if a base is still present, more acid needs to be added.
**
step 12 is not part of DS/EN 14629, but it increases the accuracy of potentiometric
titration at low chloride concentrations. If there is no chloride in the sample solution,
measurements will only produce half of the S­shaped curve. Then it is not possible
to identify the point corresponding to the disappearance of chlorides by numerical
differentiation. The added sodium chloride shall be considered, when calculating
the amount of chloride in the original sample. It is good laboratory practice to in­
clude a blind sample in the test series, i.e., a sample prepared without concrete
powder, but with 1.00 mL 0.1 M sodium chloride solution to verify the measure­
ments.
107
==PAGE:115==
Figure A.10: Addition of 10 mL nitric acid
Figure A.11: Filtration of solution
Sample preparation (water soluble chlorides)
To determine the water soluble chloride content, the chloride need to be extracted by
water.
1. Weigh the glass bottle (1)
2. Place approx. 10 g of sample in the glass bottle (1)
3. Dry the powder sample in the 105±5°C oven until constant mass
4. Register the mass of the dry sample
5. *Add 50 mL hot demineralized water and allow it to stand for 24 hours
6. Filter the solution in glass bottle (1) into the glass bottle (2) as for the acid soluble
sample preparation technique, but without rinsing with nitric acid.
7. ** Add 10 mL of concentrated nitric acid into the filtered solution in glass bottle (2)
8. *Add hot demineralised water to a total volume of 200 mL
9. Let the mixture cool
10. ***Add 1.00 mL 0.1 M sodium chloride solution
The sample preparation is essentially identical with the sample preparation described in
[ASTM C1218], with the following expectations:
*
step 5 and 8 differs from ASTM C1218 in the sense of adding hot boiled water,
where the standard prescribes bringing the solution to a boil. This is not followed
due to both convenience and to security measures.
**
step 7 differs from ASTM C1218 since it prescribes 3 mL concentrated nitric acid.
For the same reason as described in the acid soluble chloride content, 10 mL is
added in order to release the chlorides in the filtered solution.
***
step 10 is added for the same reason as for the acid soluble chloride content.
Titration
Titration is performed with the Metrhom 888 Titrando system using the Tiamo 2.5 Soft­
ware. A sub aim of this project was to create a full guide and check the new system,
108
==PAGE:116==
which has just be acquired by DTU Civil Engineering, Section for Materials and Dura­
bility. A short technical report and full guide of the use of the software can be found in
appendix B.1. The following is strictly the use of the titration equipment.
Preperation
1. Create a ”Database” and ”Method” in the Tiamo 2.5 software. Check that the stirring
is set at 6.
2. Place an empty glass beaker on the ”801 Stirrer” and insert the burette tip (silver
nitrate feeder).
3. Remove air bubbles from the system by running the ”Prepare” in ”Manual Control”.
Titration
1. Place a beaker with a sample solution on the ”801 Stirrer” and insert a clean magnet
in the beaker
2. Insert the burrette tip and Ag/AgCl electrode in the stand and adjust the height.
Check the height considering the magnet stir and the fluid height.
3. In the ”Workplace” tab, ”Run” window of the Tiamo 2.5 software, press ”Start”
4. After the titration has finished, the results can either be viewed in the “Workplace”
or in your selected “Database”.
5. Remember to thoroughly clean the electrode and burette tip with demineralized wa­
ter before performing a new titration or before leaving the equipment.
6. Repeat item 1­5 for other sample solution prepared for the test
7. From the output 1 mL has to be subtracted due to the 1 mL added NaCl
8. (After last titration) The electrode and burette tip should be inserted into the corre­
sponding storage cylinders and it is important to check that the tips are covered with
demineralized water when stored.
Expression of results
The chloride concentration is expressed as a percent of dry concrete mass for each mea­
suring point and the chloride profile can be plotted. The calculation of the apparent diffu­
sion coefficient, Dapp, and surface concentration, Cs is performed with the Excel add­in
”Solver”. The solver uses a GRG non­linear solve to minimise the sum of errors squared
to find the optimal Dapp and Cs values based on Cranks solution to Ficks 2nd law.
A detailed explanation for calculation of moisture content, mass of chlorides, chloride
concentration and the GRG non­linear solve to minimise the error of Cranks solution to
Ficks 2nd law is found in appendix D.3 along with the data obtained by the experiment.
109
==PAGE:117==
A.12
Rapid chloride migration test (RCM)
The rapid chloride migration test (or non­steady­state chloride migration) is performed ac­
cording to [NT BUILD 492: Concrete, mortar and cement­based repair materials: Chloride
migration coefficient from non­steady­state migration experiments, 1999].
Equipment
Chemicals
• Demineralized water
• Calcium hydroxide: Ca(OH)2, technical quality
• Sodium chloride: NaCl, chemical quality
• Sodium hydoxide: NaOH, chemical quality
• Silver nitrate: AgNO3 0.1M, chemical quality
Apparatus
• Water­cooled diamond saw
• Vacuum container: Capable of containing at least three speciments
• Vacuum pump: Capable of maintaining a pressure of less than 50 mbar (5 kPa) in
the container
• Compression testing machine, TONY 3000
Migration set­up (see fig. A.12)
• Silicone rubber sleeve: Inner/outer diameter 100/115 mm, about 150 mm long
• Clamp: Diameter range 105­115, 20 mm wide, stainless steel
• Catholyte reservoir: Plastic box, 370 x 270 x 280 mm
• Plastic support
• Cathode: Stainless steel plate, 0.5 mm thickness
• Anode: Stainless steel mesh, about 0.5 mm thickness
• Power supply: Capable of supplying 0­60 V DC regulated voltage with an accuracy
of ±0.1 V
• Ammeter: Capable of displaying current to ±1 mA
• Thermometer, accuracy of ±1◦C
• Spray bottle
• Slide caliper with a precision of ±0.1 mm
• Ruler with a minimum scale of 1 mm
110
==PAGE:118==
Figure A.12: Sketch of migration set­up (figure taken from NT BUILD 492).
Preparation of test specimens
Test specimen
1. Concrete cylinder (Ø100x200 mm) is cast according to appendix A.3.
2. The cylinder is cured in 20◦C tap water for the desired curing days
3. Prepare the test specimen by first cutting the cylinder into 3 slices of 50 ± 2 mm.
Avoid the outer 10 mm of the end of the cylinder due to edge effect. The end surface
that was nearer to the first cut (the middle surface) is the one to be exposed to the
chloride solution (catholyte).
4. Measure the thickness with a slide caliper and read to 0.1 mm
Preconditioning
1. After sawing, brush and wash away any burrs from the surfaces of the specimen,
and wipe off excess water from the surfaces of the specimen.
2. When the specimens are surface­dry, place them in the vacuum container for vac­
uum treatment. Both end surfaces must be exposed.
3. Reduce the absolute pressure in the vacuum container to a pressure in the range
of 10–50 mbar (1–5 kPa) within a few minutes.
4. Maintain the vacuum for three hours and then, with the vacuum pump still running,
fill the container with the saturated Ca(OH)2 solution (by dissolving an excess of
calcium hydroxide in demineralized) so as to immerse all the specimens.
5. Maintain the vacuum for a further hour before allowing air to re­enter the container.
6. Keep the specimens in the solution for 18 ± 2 hours.
Procedure
Catholyte and anolyte
1. The catholyte solution is 10 % NaCl by mass in tap water (100 g NaCl in 900 g
water, about 2 M) and the anolyte solution is 0.3 M NaOH in demineralized water
111
==PAGE:119==
(approximately 12 g NaOH in 1 litre water).
2. Store the solutions at a temperature of 20–25°C.
Temperature
1. Maintain the temperatures of the specimen and solutions in the range of 20–25 °C
during the test.
Preparation of the test
1. Fill the catholyte reservoir with about 12 litres of 10 % NaCl solution
2. Fit the rubber sleeve on the specimen and secure it with two clamps. If the curved
surface of the specimen is not smooth, or there are defects on the curved surface
which could result in significant leakage, apply a line of silicone sealant to improve
the tightness
3. Place the specimen on the plastic support in the catholyte reservoir
4. Fill the sleeve above the specimen with 300 ml anolyte solution (0.3 M NaOH)
5. Immerse the anode in the anolyte solution
6. Connect the cathode to the negative pole and the anode to the positive pole of the
power supply
Migration test
1. Turn on the power, with the voltage preset at 30 V, and record the initial current
through each specimen
2. Adjust the voltage if necessary (as shown in fig. A.13). After adjustment, note the
value of the initial current again.
3. Record the initial temperature in each anolyte solution
4. Choose an appropriate test duration according to the initial current (see fig. A.13)
5. Record the final current and temperature before terminating the test
Measurement of chloride penetration depth
1. Disassemble the specimen. A wooden rod is often helpful in removing the rubber
sleeve from the specimen
2. Rinse the specimen with tap water
3. Split the specimen axially into two pieces with the TONY 3000 compression testing
machine
4. Spray 0.1 M silver nitrate solution on to the freshly split section
5. When the white silver chloride precipitation on the split surface is clearly visible (after
about 15 minutes), measure the penetration depth, with the help of the slide calliper
and a suitable ruler, from the centre to both edges at intervals of 10 mm (see Figure
5 in Appendix 1) to obtain seven depths. Measure the depth to an accuracy of 0.1
mm
112
==PAGE:120==
• If the penetration front to be measured is obviousl blocked by the aggregate,
move the measurement to the nearest front where there is no significant block­
ing by aggregate or, alternatively, ignore this depth if there are more than five
valid depths
• If there is a significant defect in the specimen which results in a penetration front
much larger than the average, ignore this front as indicative of the penetration
depth, but note and report the condition
• To obviate the edge effect due to a non­homogeneous degree of saturation
or possible leakage, do not make any depth measurements in the zone within
about 10 mm from the edge
Figure A.13: Test voltage and duration for concrete specimen with normal binder content
(table taken from NT BUILD 492).
Figure A.14: Illustration of measurement for chloride penetration depths (taken from NT
BUILD 492)
113
==PAGE:121==

The non­steady­state migration coefficient can be calculated from equation

1 −2cd
c0
)
(A.8)
Where
Dnssm
is the non­steady­state migration coefficient, in m2/s
z
is the absolute value of ion valence for chloride, z = 1
F
is the Faraday constant, F = 9.648 · 104 J/(V·mol)
U
is the absolute value of the applied voltage, in V
R
is the gas constant, R = 8.314 J/(K·mol)
T
is the average value of the initial an final temperatures in
the anolyte solution, in K
L
is the thickness of the specimen, in m
xd
is the average value of the penetration depths, m
t
is the test duration, in s
erf−1
is the inverse error function
cd
is the chloride concentration at which the colour changes, cd ≈0.07 M
c0
is the chloride concentration in the catholyte solution, c0 ≈2 M.

B.1
New titration equipment: Setup, user manual and method analysis of Methrom 888
